God's Will and Friendship with Enemies
Maturidi presents an argument from the witnessed world against God willing friendship with His enemies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God cannot will friendship with those who choose enmity against Him, based on analogy from the witnessed world.

Core Idea
God is not weak, so He cannot will friendship with His enemies.

Maturidi begins by drawing an analogy from human experience. In this world, anyone who seeks friendship with an enemy does so out of weakness or fear. Since God is all-powerful and independent, it is impossible for Him to will friendship with Iblis and those who choose enmity against Him. This establishes a foundational point about God's will and His relationship with His creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi starts with 'And also' to connect to previous arguments.
The passage uses a clear analogy (qiyās) from the witnessed world to the unseen.
2
Understand the analogy
Identify the human situation: willing friendship with an enemy implies weakness or fear. Maturidi uses this as a premise.
Think about why a powerful person would not seek friendship with an enemy.
3
Apply the analogy to God
Since God is absolutely powerful and free from need, He cannot will friendship with those who are His enemies.
This argument reinforces God's perfection and independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن الذي عليه أمر الشاهد أن كل من أراد موالاة من يختار عدواته، كان يكون عن ضعف وخوف، فلا يجوز أن يكون الله تعالى يريد موالاة إبليس والذين اختاروا عداوته. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا إن شرط كل من فعله اختيار [هو] الإرادة، وكل من فعله الاضطرار [ف]إنه غير مريد لذلك. فلو كان الله لفعل العبد غير مريد ليكون على ما كان يكون مضطرا؛ ولذلك لا يجوز أن يكون لأحد في فعل غيره إرادة، لما لا يحتمل خروجه على ما يريد، وسموا ذلك تمنيا؛ فعلى ذلك لو توهم كون شيء لم يرده الله كانت إرادته تخرج مخرج التمني. وأيضا إنه لو جعل لنا أن نبوة نبي بقول بشر، يكون ذلك معصية لنا أن نريده، من حيث
And also what is the case in the witnessed world: that everyone who wills friendship with one who chooses enmity against him, would be out of weakness and fear, so it is not permissible for Allah the Exalted to will friendship with Iblis and those who chose His enmity. And there is no power except with Allah. And also the condition of everyone whose act is choice is that [he has] will, and everyone whose act is compulsion [then] he is not willing that. So if Allah were for the act of the servant not willing, then it would be that he is compelled; and therefore it is not permissible for anyone to have will in the act of another, because it does not admit of coming out upon what he wills, and they call that a wish; so upon that, if one imagined the existence of something that God did not will, His will would come out as a wish. And also if it were made for us that the prophethood of a prophet by the saying of a human, that would be a sin for us to will it, from where
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage contains three arguments. First, Maturidi argues from the witnessed world: anyone who desires friendship with someone who chooses enmity does so out of weakness and fear; it is not permissible for God to will friendship with Iblis and those who choose enmity against Him. This is because God is all-powerful and independent. Second, he argues about will and compulsion: the condition for an act of choice is that the agent has will; an act of compulsion lacks will. If God were not willing the act of the servant, then the servant would be compelled, and it would not be permissible for anyone to have will in another's act, because the outcome may not match the will. People call such ineffective willing 'wishing' (tamannī). Thus, if one imagines something existing that God did not will, then God's will would be like a wish—ineffective. Third, Maturidi begins an argument about prophethood: if prophethood were established by a human saying, it would be a sin for us to will it. The passage ends abruptly at 'from where'.

Key Terms
الموالاة (al-muwālāh)
العداوة (al-‘adāwah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God can will friendship with anyone, including those who oppose Him, as a sign of mercy.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that willing friendship with an enemy implies a need that contradicts God's absolute independence. God's mercy is not based on weakness.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it not permissible for God to will friendship with Iblis and those who choose enmity against Him?
A) Because they are too sinful
B) Because it would be out of weakness and fear
C) Because God does not love them
D) Because they have free will

Key Takeaway
God's will is perfect and independent; He cannot will friendship with His enemies because that would imply weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This lesson shows that God cannot will friendship with His enemies because that would imply imperfection. How does your own pursuit of human approval reveal a gap between what you profess about God's strength and how you actually live?

Will, Compulsion, and Wish in Divine Action
Maturidi distinguishes between will and compulsion, defines wish, and begins an argument about prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between will and compulsion, and why God's will must be effective, not a mere wish.

Core Idea
God's will is always effective; a will that fails is just a wish.

Maturidi continues by examining the nature of will and compulsion. He argues that an act of choice requires will, while an act of compulsion lacks it. If God were not willing the servant's act, then the servant would be compelled. Furthermore, if someone's will does not bring about its object, it is not truly will but mere wish (tamannī). This argument reinforces that God's will is always effective and never like a wish. The passage also touches on a final incomplete argument about prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the structure: first a definition, then a conditional, then a conclusion about wish.
The passage contains logical reasoning: if-then statements.
2
Understand choice vs compulsion
Identify the key terms: ikhtiyār (choice) and idtirār (compulsion). Maturidi states that an act of choice necessarily involves will, while an act of compulsion does not.
Think about how this applies to human actions.
3
Grasp the concept of wish
If someone's will does not result in the intended act, it is called tamannī (wish). Maturidi applies this to God: if something existed that God did not will, His will would be like a wish.
This shows that God's will is always effective and never a mere wish.
4
Reflect on the incomplete argument
The passage ends with a partial argument about prophethood. Consider what Maturidi might have been developing.
The text is cut off; we can't complete it, but notice the connection to human will and divine approval.

God’s Will is Perfectly Aligned with His Knowledge
Maturidi’s reductio on divine will and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God cannot will something that contradicts His knowledge, and how this affirms divine perfection.

Core Idea
God’s will is flawless—it cannot desire what He knows won’t happen.

This passage from Imam al-Maturidi presents a powerful reductio argument: if God’s will were to aim at something other than what He knows will occur, that would imply fault—a logical impossibility for the Divine. As you read, notice how Maturidi uses the concepts of lying and foolishness to show the harmony between God’s will and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation once. Identify the conditional statement: 'if God willed X, then He would be Y.'
Notice the structure: a supposition leads to an absurd conclusion.
2
Analyze the reductio
Maturidi assumes a false premise (that God could will differently) and derives a contradiction (God would be a lying fool).
This technique is common in kalam.
3
Understand the implications
Since the conclusion is impossible for God, the premise must be false. Thus God only wills what He knows will happen.
This establishes the harmony of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله تعالى لو أراد أن يكون الكفر والشر غير الذي يعلم أنه يكون، غير الذي أخبر أنه يكون، لكان أراد أن يكون كذاباً سفيهاً، ومن تلك إرادته لم يجز أن يكون إلهاً ورباً. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, if God Most High wanted disbelief and evil to be other than what He knows it will be, other than what He has informed that it will be, He would have wanted to be a lying fool, and from such will it is not permissible for Him to be a God and Lord. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage presents a concise reductio ad absurdum. If God's will were to bring about something different from what His knowledge knows will happen and what His speech has informed will happen, then His will would be aimed at making Him a liar (kadhdhāb) and a fool (safīh). Since these are impossible for God, the supposition is false. Therefore God's will must align perfectly with His knowledge and information, and He only wills what He knows will occur.

Key Terms
الكذب
السفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could will something other than what He knows will happen.
Correction: That would imply deficiency in God, so it's impossible. God's will always accords with His knowledge.

Checkpoint
What would be the consequence if God willed something other than what He knows will happen?
A) He would be described as powerless.
B) He would be described as a liar and a fool.
C) His knowledge would increase.
D) Nothing would change.

Key Takeaway
God’s will is in perfect harmony with His knowledge—He only wills what He knows will occur, preserving His perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself wanting something you know God has decreed will not happen? How does this lesson challenge the way you pray and surrender to His will? Commit today to a specific area where you will align your desires with what you know of His knowledge and decree.
?
When you face evil or suffering, do you secretly doubt that God's will is perfectly aligned with His knowledge? How would your heart change if you truly believed that His will cannot be ignorant or foolish? Identify one painful event where you will consciously affirm this truth.
?
If God's will is necessarily in harmony with His knowledge, then your free choices are never a surprise to Him. Does that comfort you or unsettle you? What does it mean for your personal responsibility and your trust in His plan? Write one decision you will make today with that assurance.

Maturidi's Arguments for the Effectiveness of Divine Will
Maturidi uses the analogy of building and the definition of error to refute the idea of God's will being ineffective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's two arguments (from wisdom and from the nature of error) that demonstrate that God's will cannot be ineffective.

Core Idea
God's will is always effective; inefficiency would imply ignorance or frivolity.

Maturidi presents two compelling arguments to affirm that God's will is perfectly effective. First, he uses the analogy of a builder to show that intending an outcome different from what occurs indicates either ignorance or frivolity, both impossible for God. Then, he analyzes the concept of error itself, showing that if God's actions failed to align with His intention, they would be erroneous. This passage firmly establishes that Allah's will is always realized.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the rhetorical question about building.
2
Understand the wisdom argument
Identify the core claim: if someone intends one outcome but another results, they are either ignorant or frivolous. Apply this to God, who is beyond these.
The building analogy illustrates frivolity.
3
Understand the error argument
Note the definition of error in human experience: two types. Then apply the logic to God: if His actions did not match His intention, they would be error.
Error here is not sin but misalignment.
4
Connect the two arguments
See how both arguments lead to the same conclusion: God's will is perfectly effective. The wisdom argument focuses on intent vs outcome; the error argument uses the definition of error.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider what this teaches about divine attributes: knowledge, will, and power are perfectly aligned. This refutes any notion of weakness or ignorance in God.
This connects to broader Aqeedah discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً في الحكمة أن كل من صنع أمراً يريد غير الذي يكون به، كان يكون جاهلاً بالعواقب أو عابثاً بالفعل، والله تعالى يجل عن هذين الوصفين. ألا يرى أن من بنى لشيء يعلم أنه لا يكون كان ذلك منه عبثاً، ولو كان به شيء غير الذي يريده كان جاهلاً به. وأيضاً إن الخطأ المعروف في الشاهد نوعان أحدهما خروج الفعل على تقدير يجهله؛ والثاني وقوعه في غير الذي يريده. فلو كان الله تعالى يريد بما أعطى غير الذي يكون به لكان يكون فعله خطأ، على ما عرفنا الله تعالى من فعل الخطأ. وبالله التوفيق.
And also, in wisdom, that whoever does a thing intending other than what results from it, he would be ignorant of consequences or frivolous in action, and God Most High is exalted above these two descriptions. Do you not see that whoever builds for something that he knows will not happen, that from him is frivolity, and if there were something in it other than what he intends, he would be ignorant of it. And also, error known in the witnessed world is of two types: one is the action occurring according to an estimation that he is ignorant of; the second is its occurrence in other than what he intends. So if God Most High intended by what He gives other than what results from it, His action would be error, according to what God Most High has taught us of the action of error. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage combines two arguments. The first argument is from wisdom (ḥikma): if a person intends one thing but something else results, he is either ignorant of the outcome or acting frivolously (ʿābith). God is transcendent above both. The analogy of building something that one knows will not be used demonstrates futility. The second argument defines error (khaṭaʾ) in human experience as having two forms: (1) when the action occurs based on a mistaken estimation, and (2) when it results in something other than what was intended. If God's will were such that the outcome differed from His intention, then His act would be error, which is impossible for God. Thus God's will must be perfectly effective.

Key Terms
الحكمة
العابث
الخطأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument limits God's power by suggesting He cannot will differently from what occurs.
Correction: The argument actually affirms that God's will and knowledge are perfectly aligned; it does not limit power but asserts that God's actions are never futile or erroneous.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument in this passage, how many types of error exist in the witnessed world?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that if God's will could be ineffective, His actions would be either frivolous or erroneous, which is impossible for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where your plans did not work out as you intended, do you truly believe that God's will for that situation was effective and perfect, or do you harbor resentment as if something went wrong? How can you train your heart to accept outcomes as exactly what God willed, even when they disappoint you?
?
Consider an area of your life where you repeatedly act without clear purpose or with negligence, like the frivolous builder. What does Maturidi's analogy teach you about the seriousness of your intentions? How can you align your actions with the wisdom that God expects from His servants?
?
If error is defined as a mismatch between intention and outcome, and God is free from error, what does that imply about your own attempts to control results? Are there ways in which you try to force outcomes that you should entrust to God's perfect will instead?

God's Will and Friendship with Enemies
Maturidi argues that God cannot will to ally with those who choose enmity, as that would imply weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi uses an analogy from human social behavior to deny that God wills friendship with His enemies.

Core Idea
God's will is too perfect to include friendship with those who choose enmity.

This brief but powerful argument from Imam Maturidi uses a simple observation about human behavior to clarify a point about God's will. In everyday life, if someone tries to befriend a person who is openly hostile, we assume they are acting from weakness or fear. Maturidi applies this principle to God, showing that such a motivation cannot apply to the All-Powerful. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the concise argument structure.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the analogy from the witnessed world
Identify the premise: In human experience, willing friendship with an enemy stems from weakness or fear. This is an observational truth.
The 'witnessed world' (shāhid) is a key concept in kalam.
3
Apply the principle to God
Since God is absolutely powerful and free from all deficiencies, He cannot be described as acting from weakness. Therefore, He cannot will friendship with those who choose enmity.
This is an argument by analogy (qiyās al-ghāʿib ʿalā al-shāhid).
4
Draw the theological conclusion
The conclusion is that God's will does not extend to approving enmity or establishing friendship with enemies. Rather, He wills the creation of their actions without approving them.
This guards against the misconception that God's will of all events means He approves of them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن الذي عليه أمر الشاهد أن كل من أراد موالاة من يختار عدواته، كان يكون عن ضعف وخوف، فلا يجوز أن يكون الله تعالى يريد موالاة إبليس والذين اختاروا عداوته. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, the matter in the witnessed world is that whoever wills to befriend someone who chooses enmity towards him, that would be from weakness and fear, so it is not permissible that God Most High wills to befriend Iblis and those who have chosen enmity towards Him. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This short proof draws an analogy from human social behavior. In the witnessed world (shāhid), if a person wants to befriend (muwālāt) someone who is actively hostile, the motive is either weakness or fear. Since God is absolutely powerful and free from deficiency, He cannot be described as willing friendship with Iblis and other disbelievers who have chosen enmity. Therefore, God's will cannot be to make them friends; rather, His will encompasses the creation of their acts of enmity, not approval of them.

Key Terms
الموالاة
العداوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God wills all things, He must also will friendship with disbelievers or approve of their enmity.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God's will to create an act is not the same as willing it as an act of obedience or friendship. God's will encompasses creation, not approval of sin.

Checkpoint
What does the analogy from human behavior in this passage prove about God's will?
A) God wills everything including friendship with disbelievers.
B) God cannot will to befriend those who are hostile because that would indicate weakness.
C) God is free to will anything without restriction.
D) Human behavior has no bearing on understanding God.

Key Takeaway
God's perfect power and freedom from deficiency mean He cannot will friendship with those who choose enmity, as that would imply weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If God's will is too perfect to include friendship with those who choose enmity, what does that say about your own choices? Are you living in a way that aligns with God's friendship, or are you choosing enmity through your actions? Commit today to examine your heart and turn away from what incurs God's displeasure.

The Example of Iblis: Will Cannot Contradict Knowledge
Maturidi uses Iblis to illustrate that divine will cannot contradict divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for God to will something He knows will not occur, and how the example of Iblis illustrates this principle.

Core Idea
What Allah knows will not happen cannot be willed—even by Allah.

Imam Maturidi presents a subtle but powerful argument about the relationship between divine knowledge and will. Using the well-known case of Iblis—whose eternal damnation was known to Allah—he shows that it would be absurd to attribute to Allah a will for something He knows will never occur. This principle is derived from a rational analogy with human obligations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then follow the literal English translation. Notice the logical flow from the example of Iblis to the general principle.
2
Identify the analogy
Maturidi draws an analogy between human and divine will. Identify the premise: we cannot pray for guidance for Iblis because we know it won't happen.
3
Understand the implication for God
If we humans are not even obligated to will something we know impossible, how could God—who knows all—will something impossible? This step is key.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with a reminder that all power is from God. Contemplate how this argument safeguards divine attributes from contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمثله إذ علمنا الله أنه أخبر عن ذلك وأنه علم أن لا يكون، كان له أن لا يريد في الحكمة. على أنه لا يختلف [في] أن ليس للعبد أن يسأل الله هداية من يعلم أنه لا يهتدى نحو إبليس، لم يكن له أن يقول: اللهم اهده، لما يعلم أنه لا يكون، ثم محال أن يكون علينا إرادة ذلك؛ وإذ لم يكن علينا إرادة ما نعلم أنه لا يكون لم يجز أن يقال ذلك على الله؛ إذ كونها [جائزا] علينا إنما يكون إذا جهلنا بحاله. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
So for example, since we know from God that He informed about that and that He knew that it will not be, He had [the option] not to will it in wisdom. And it is not disputed that the servant is not to ask God for guidance for one whom he knows will not be guided, like Iblis; he may not say: 'O God, guide him,' because he knows that it will not be. Then it is impossible that we should be obligated to will that; and since we are not obligated to will what we know will not be, it is not permissible to say that concerning God; for its being permissible for us is only when we are ignorant of its state. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy (qiyās) from the human to the divine. Maturidi begins with a hypothetical: we know from God's own report that He knows a certain thing will never occur. In wisdom, He may therefore not will it. Then he states a principle universally agreed: a servant cannot pray for guidance for someone like Iblis whom he knows will never be guided, because such a prayer would request something known impossible. Since we humans are not obligated to will what we know cannot happen, it follows that we cannot attribute such a will to God either, because the only reason it would be permissible for us to will something is ignorance of its impossibility. The passage concludes by affirming that all power is from God. The argument establishes a boundary for divine will: it cannot contradict divine knowledge.

Key Terms
إرادة
علم
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God can will something to happen that He knows will never happen.
Correction: This passage shows that divine will is necessarily consistent with divine knowledge. It is impossible for God to will something He knows will not occur.

Checkpoint
Why cannot a servant pray for guidance for Iblis according to Maturidi?
A) Because Iblis is beyond forgiveness
B) Because the servant knows Iblis will never be guided
C) Because God forbids praying for devils
D) Because Iblis has free will

Key Takeaway
What Allah knows will never happen cannot be willed—whether by human or by Allah—because will is contingent on possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Four Meanings of Mashī'ah (Will) in Relation to God
Maturidi enumerates four conventional meanings of will and explains which apply to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four conventional meanings of mashī'ah (will) and which ones are applicable to Allah, according to Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
God's will is creative determination, not wish or approval.

Imam Maturidi teaches us that the word 'will' has multiple meanings in everyday language. When we speak of God's will, we must be precise about which meaning is intended. This lesson explores four conventional meanings and clarifies which ones are affirmed for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two approaches
The passage begins by presenting two approaches when asked about God's willing the acts of unbelievers. Notice the condition for each approach.
3
List the four conventional meanings
Find the four meanings enumerated: wish, command, approval, and negation of being overcome. Write them down in order.
4
Note which are negated and why
For the first three meanings, note that Maturidi explicitly negates them from God in certain contexts. Understand the reasons given.
5
Understand the affirmed meaning
The fourth meaning is affirmed: God's will as His determination without compulsion. See how the passage concludes by affirming God's power and freedom from coercion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل عندنا - إذا سئلنا عن مشيئة الله فعل الكفرة على ما كان [عليه] وجهان. أحدهما القول بذلك في الإطلاق على ما عرف من الإرادة في ذلك. والثاني منع الإطلاق إذا لم يفهم مراد السائل أو خشي أن يريد التعنت في ذلك، وهو أن يقال: إن للمشيئة معاني فيما يتعارف. أحدها التمني، وذلك عن الله منفي في كل شيء. والثاني الأمر والدعاء إليه، فذلك منفي عن الله في كل فعل يذم فاعله والثالث الرضا به والقبول له، وذلك كذلك أيضا في كل فعل يذم عليه. والرابع تأويله بنفي الغلبة وخروج الفعل على ما يقدره ويريده، وهذا نقول ذلك، وقد أجمع على معناه. فمن أنكر ذلك بعد إعطاء معناه فهو قدر المشيئة على غير حقيقة المراد منها. وهو عندنا لازم [في حقه تعالى، إذ هو لكل شيء خالق، وقد ثبت وصفه فيما يخلقه أنه غير مضطر إليه ولا مكره عليه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the principle with us—when we are asked about God's will (mashī'ah) concerning the actions of the unbelievers—there are two aspects. One is to affirm it absolutely, according to the known meaning of will in that regard. The second is to refrain from absolute affirmation if the intent of the questioner is not understood, or if one fears that he seeks obstinacy. And it is to be said: Indeed mashī'ah has conventionally known meanings. First: wish (tamannī), and that is negated from God in every thing. Second: command (amr) and invitation to it, and that is negated from God concerning every act whose doer is blameworthy. Third: approval (riḍā) and acceptance, and that is likewise negated concerning every act for which one is blamed. Fourth: its interpretation by negating being overcome and the act proceeding according to what He determines and wills, and this is what we say. And its meaning is agreed upon. So whoever denies that after its meaning is given, he has defined mashī'ah in a way not corresponding to its true intended meaning. And it is necessary with us concerning Him, may He be exalted, for He is the Creator of everything, and it is established that His description concerning what He creates is that He is neither coerced nor compelled in it. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the meanings of mashī'ah (will) and a statement of the Sunni position. Maturidi first notes two approaches when asked about God's willing the acts of unbelievers: either affirm it absolutely or withhold if the questioner is hostile. Then he enumerates four conventional meanings of will: (1) wish (tamannī) – impossible for God; (2) command (amr) – negated for blameworthy acts; (3) approval (riḍā) – also negated for evil acts; (4) the absence of being overcome and the act proceeding according to one's determination and desire – this is the meaning affirmed for God. The fourth meaning is agreed upon. Maturidi warns that if someone acknowledges this meaning but still denies the term, he is distorting the true referent. The passage ends by affirming God's creative power and that He is free from any compulsion.

Key Terms
مشيئة
تمني
أمر
رضا
غلبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God's will is like human desire or approval, implying that God wishes for things He cannot have or approves of evil.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that God's will is neither wish, command, nor approval in the sense of desiring or approving evil. Rather, it is His effective determination that brings about creation without any compulsion.

Checkpoint

A) Wish (tamannī)
B) Command (amr)
C) Approval (riḍā)
D) Negation of being overcome (nafy al-ghalabah)

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms that God's will is His creative determination, free from any compulsion, and distinct from wish, command, or approval.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How must you retrain your heart to accept that God's will is perfect determination, free from any compulsion or deficiency?

Distinguishing Divine Will from Love and Approval
Maturidi refutes the equation of divine will with love and approval.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's willing evil acts does not mean He loves or approves of them, and the wisdom behind His willing the existence of enemies.

Core Idea
God's will for existence does not equal His approval.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi addresses a subtle objection: if God wills evil, does that mean He loves it? The opponent had argued that will and approval are inseparable. Maturidi responds by distinguishing between God's creative will (irādat kawn) and His moral approval. He also shows that there is profound wisdom in allowing evil to exist — it manifests God's complete self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent claims that if God wills something, He must love and approve of it. See how Maturidi counters this by separating the concepts of will and approval.
Notice that Maturidi first gives a positive reason for why God wills evil acts from enemies.
2
Examine the wisdom behind willing evil
Maturidi explains that God wills the existence of an enemy to show His wealth (self-sufficiency) and to remove any notion of weakness. Read the verse he cites.
3
The direct refutation
Maturidi explicitly rejects saying God loves Iblis or filth, even though He willed their existence. Understand that irādat kawn does not imply maḥabbah or riḍā.
This is the key takeaway of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه إذا أراد لمن علم أن يختار هو عدواته أن يكون منه: بأن يكون عدواً له، فهذا ليزول معنى الضعف ويظهر الغنى عنه وعن فعله، كما قال: {إن الله لغني عن العالمين}. على أنه يزعم أن معنى الإرادة أن لا يُغلب، وقد وجد في هذا، فليقل ما شاء، فهو له في الأول جواب. وأما جوابه بالمحبة والرضا فإنه لا يجوز أن يقال: إن الله يحب إبليس ويرضى به وكذلك الخبائث والأقذار، وإن كان أراد كونهم، فمثله فعل الكفر وكذا كل قبائح الصور والجواهر. والله أعلم.
And after that, when He wills that from whom He knows will choose enmity that it be from him: that he be an enemy to Him, then this is so that the meaning of weakness disappears and the wealth [self-sufficiency] from him and from his action appears, as He said: {Indeed God is free from need of the worlds}. Moreover, he claims that the meaning of will is that He not be overcome, and this has occurred in this [case], so let him say what he wills, for him there is a response in the first. As for his response by love and approval, it is not permissible to say that God loves Iblis and is pleased with him, and likewise impure things and filth, even if He willed their existence; similarly the act of disbelief and all ugly forms and essences. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage continues the objection-and-answer. The opponent (Ka'bi) had argued that if God wills evil actions, then He must love and approve them. Maturidi refutes this by first explaining the wisdom in God's willing the existence of an enemy: it manifests God's self-sufficiency and lack of weakness. He then points out that the opponent's definition of will as 'not being overcome' actually applies here (since God's will for the enemy's existence is fulfilled). Then Maturidi directly addresses the claim that will implies love and approval: it is absolutely not permissible to say God loves Iblis or filth, even though He wills their existence. Similarly, disbelief and ugly forms are willed for existence but not loved. Thus the opponent's equation of will with love and approval is false.

Key Terms
محبة (maḥabbah)
رضا (riḍā)
إرادة كون (irādat kawn)
غنى (ghinā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills something, He must love and approve of it.
Correction: God's will for existence (irādat kawn) does not entail His love or approval; He may will evil for wise purposes while remaining free of any deficiency.

Key Takeaway
God's will for existence (irādat kawn) does not imply His love or approval; He wills evil for wise purposes while remaining free of any deficiency.

Not-Willing Implies Displeasure, Not Deficiency
Maturidi answers a self-objection about will and deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between divine displeasure and deficiency in response to the objection that not-willing something would imply a lack in God.

Core Idea
Not-willing does not mean lacking; it means disapproving.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a subtle objection: if something being outside God's knowledge would be ignorance, then something being outside God's will must be a deficiency. But Maturidi clarifies that absence of will does not imply a defect in God; it only implies displeasure (karāhah). This distinction preserves God's perfection while allowing for moral distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection as Maturidi poses it
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice that Maturidi first states the opponent's analogy: just as things outside God's knowledge imply ignorance (a deficiency), so things outside God's will should imply deficiency (incapacity).
2
Notice Maturidi's precise answer
Maturidi responds succinctly: 'It only entails displeasure, not deficiency.' This is the core of the argument. Understand the distinction between a defect in God's nature and an attitude of disapproval.
The Arabic word for displeasure is 'كراهة' (karāhah).
3
Reflect on the phrase 'كراهة النهي كذا'
The passage ends with 'The displeasure of prohibition is such.' This likely means that God's displeasure is analogous to a prohibition in law—it expresses disapproval without implying any imperfection in the one who prohibits.
This phrase may continue, but the argument is clear: God's not-willing is a matter of divine wisdom, not deficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بما يعقب خروج الشيء عن علمه جهلاً، لم لا أوجب خروجه عن إرادته نقصانا، وهو عجز؟ فقال: إنما يعقب الكراهة لا النقصان. قيل: كراهة النهي كذا.
Then he objected himself by what follows from something's leaving His knowledge: ignorance. Why does it not necessitate its leaving His will being a deficiency, which is incapacity? So he said: 'It only entails displeasure, not deficiency.' It is said: 'The displeasure of prohibition is such.'
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This is a final objection-answer pair. The opponent (or a self-objection) argues: if something is outside God's will, that implies a deficiency (like incapacity), just as if something were outside His knowledge, that would be ignorance. The answer is that not-willing leads to displeasure (karāhah), not to a defect in God. The objection incorrectly attempts to analogize between knowledge and will: absence of will does not imply ignorance or inability; it implies a different attitude. The phrase 'The displeasure of prohibition is such' likely means that the displeasure is consistent with God's wisdom. The passage is cut off, but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
علم ('ilm)
جهل (jahl)
إرادة (irādah)
كراهة (karāhah)
نقصان (nuqsān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That not-willing something implies a deficiency in God (like ignorance or incapacity).
Correction: Maturidi explains that not-willing only implies displeasure, not a defect. God's will is not a measure of His power but of His approval.

Key Takeaway

Why God Wills Evil: Eliminating Weakness
Maturidi presents the purpose of God's will for evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God wills the evil acts of His enemies to manifest His self-sufficiency and eliminate any notion of weakness.

Core Idea
God's willing of evil proves His strength, not His weakness.

Maturidi opens by explaining that God's will for an enemy's enmity is not arbitrary but serves a purpose: to show that God is free from need of that enemy's obedience. This refutes any suggestion that God is weak or dependent. The passage sets the stage for deeper discussion on divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi frames the purpose of God's willing of enmity.
The phrase 'ليزول معنى الضعف' is key.
2
Identify the opponent's view on will
Find where Maturidi mentions the opponent's definition of will as 'not being overwhelmed'. Understand how Maturidi counters that.
3
Reflect on the Quranic citation
Consider the verse 'إن الله لغني عن العالمين' and how it supports Maturidi's point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه إذا أراد لمن علم أن يختار هو عدواته أن يكون منه بأن يكون عدواً لله، فهذا ليزول معنى الضعف ويظهر الغنى عنه وعن فعله، كما قال: إن الله لغني عن العالمين [العنكبوت ٦/٢٩]. على أنه يزعم أن معنى الإرادة أن لا يُغلب، وقد وجد في هذا، فليقل ما شاء، فهو له في الأول جواب. وأما جوابه بالمحبة والرضا فإنه لا يجوز أن يقال: إن الله يحب إبليس ويرضى به وكذلك الخبائث والأقذار، وإن كان أراد كونهم، فمثله فعل الكفر وكذا كل قبائح الصور والجواهر. والله أعلم. وأجاب لما عورض من الزيادة في ملكه ما لا يريده بالرضا والمحبة، وقد بينا التفريق في ذلك بما هو فعله. ثم قال: إذا قدر على المنع فلم يمنع فليس بممنوع. فيقال له: لو قدر وهو لا يريده ليمنع، فدل كونه بلا إرادة أنه لم يقدر. ومما يبين ذلك أنه لو قهرهم على الإسلام لم يكونوا مسلمين، قهرا، يبيّن أنه لم يكن يقدر على ذلك، وذلك حق الغلبة والقهر في الشاهد. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعارض بتركهم، فيقال: ليس في الترك خلاف له في الإرادة، فيلحق ما ذكرنا من الزيادة في ملكه ما لا يريده. وبالله التوفيق. وعارض بمثله في الشاهد، وهو خطأ لوجهين أحدهما أن ملكنا لا يقدر على المنع، وإلا كان يمنع عن كل شيء لم يرده. والثاني أن ذلك ليس في ملكه ولا سلطانه، لما ليس لملك الأرض على أفعال غيره ملك ولا سلطان. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And after, if He willed for the one He knows will choose enmity to be an enemy to God, then this is so that the meaning of weakness disappears and His self-sufficiency from him and from his action appears, as He said: 'Indeed Allah is free from need of the worlds.' Despite his claim that the meaning of will is that one not be overwhelmed, and this has been found in this, so let him say what he wishes, for it is for him the first answer. As for his answer with love and pleasure, it is not permissible to say that Allah loves Iblis and is pleased with him, and likewise vile things and filth, even if He willed their existence. Similar is the act of disbelief, and likewise every ugly form and substance. And Allah knows best. And he answered when it was objected concerning the increase in His kingdom of what He does not will, with pleasure and love, and we have shown the distinction in that by what is His action. Then he said: If he has power to prevent and did not prevent, then it is not prevented. So it is said to him: If he had power while he does not will it, he would prevent, so the fact that it is without will indicates that he did not have power. And among that which clarifies this is that if he compelled them to Islam, they would not be Muslims, by compulsion. It shows that he was not able to do that, and that is the truth of overwhelming and coercion in the witnessed realm. And there is no power except with Allah. And he objected with their leaving, so it is said: There is no opposition to Him in will in leaving, so what we mentioned of increase in His kingdom of what He does not will attaches. And with Allah is success. And he objected with something similar in the witnessed realm, and it is erroneous for two reasons: one, that our king cannot prevent, otherwise he would prevent everything he does not will. Second, that this is not in his possession or authority, because the earthly king has no possession or authority over the actions of others. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about divine will and prevention. Maturidi begins by addressing the claim that God's will for an enemy's enmity is to eliminate any notion of weakness in God. He then refutes the opponent's (Ka'bi's) use of love and pleasure as synonymous with will, stating that one cannot say God loves or is pleased with Iblis or vile things. The opponent then argues that if God had power to prevent an act but did not, then the act is not prevented (i.e., it is willed). Maturidi responds: if God had power and did not will to prevent, He would have prevented; therefore, the absence of prevention without will shows lack of power. He gives the example of compelling people to Islam: if God forced them, they would not be true Muslims, indicating God could not bring about genuine faith by force. Then the opponent objects by citing cases where God leaves people to their own choices. Maturidi replies that leaving is not opposition to will; but if leaving implies no will, then we would have the earlier problem of increasing God's kingdom with what He does not will. Finally, the opponent analogizes to human kings. Maturidi rejects this for two reasons: first, a human king cannot prevent everything he dislikes; second, a human king has no authority over the actions of others. The argument proceeds step by step, using logical reasoning and Quranic principles.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
ضعف (daʿf)
غنى (ghina)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's willing of evil makes Him weak or dependent on creation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that willing evil actually demonstrates God's strength and independence, as He is not compelled by anyone.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the purpose of God willing an enemy's enmity?
A) To show that God is weak and needs the enemy.
B) To eliminate the meaning of weakness and manifest God's self-sufficiency.
C) To force the enemy to obey Him.
D) To prove that God is not all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
God's will for evil acts is not a sign of weakness but a demonstration of His absolute independence and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where someone opposes you or does evil against you, do you feel that God's will is somehow compromised? How does Maturidi's argument that God's willing of evil shows His self-sufficiency change the way you view your own trials?
?
Are you willing to accept that God's will includes enmity towards Him, and that this is actually a demonstration of His independence? Where in your heart do you resist this idea, and why?
?
The passage says God wills the enemy's enmity to remove weakness from Himself. Do you believe that God would will any act of evil, even your own sins? How does that confront your understanding of repentance and accountability?

Will versus Approval: God's Relation to Evil
Maturidi distinguishes between will and love/pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi insists that God can will the existence of evil without loving or approving of it, distinguishing between creative will and divine pleasure.

Core Idea
God may will what He does not love.

Maturidi now confronts an opponent who equates will with love and pleasure. He argues that it is impermissible to say God loves Iblis or filth, even though God willed their existence. This distinction is crucial for preserving divine transcendence and justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of love and pleasure
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note the strong language: 'لا يجوز أن يقال' (it is not permissible to say).
2
Identify what God can will without loving
Maturidi lists Iblis, vile things, disbelief, and ugly forms. Understand that these are created by God's will but are not objects of His love.
3
Understand the objection about increase in kingdom
The opponent argues that if God's kingdom includes what He does not will, that would be an increase. Maturidi responds that the distinction is in God's action.
This connects to the earlier argument.

Source Text

A) Yes, because God willed his existence.
B) No, it is not permissible, even though God willed his existence.
C) Only if Iblis repents.
D) Yes, but only metaphorically.

God's will to create evil does not entail His love or approval; these are separate attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face evil or suffering in your life, do you ever assume that because God willed it, He must be pleased with your pain? How does this lesson free you from that false conclusion, and what can you now tell yourself when hardships arise?
?
Think of a person you know who is deep in sin. Have you secretly doubted God's goodness because He allowed that person to continue? How does the distinction between God's will and His love change the way you pray for them and view their situation?
?
Are there desires in your heart that you have justified because 'God put them there' or 'He must want this for me'? How might you be confusing your own will with God's approval? What step can you take today to submit your desires to God's revealed pleasure, not just His permission?

The Prevention Argument: Power and Will
Maturidi refutes the prevention argument and uses compulsion example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that God's failure to prevent evil implies He willed it, and how the example of forced conversion supports his argument.

Core Idea
Non-prevention without will shows lack of power, not permission.

The opponent argues that if God had the power to prevent evil but did not, then the evil is not prevented, implying God willed it. Maturidi counters that if God had power and did not will the evil, He would have prevented it; thus non-prevention indicates impotence, not permission. He illustrates with the example of forced conversion.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument
Identify the conditional: 'إذا قدر على المنع فلم يمنع فليس بممنوع'.
2
Understand Maturidi's response
Maturidi inverts the logic: if God had power and did not will evil, He would have prevented it. So the absence of prevention shows either lack of power or will. The opponent denies will, so it must be lack of power.
3
Analyze the example of compulsion
Maturidi uses the example of forcing people to Islam: if God compelled them, they would not be true Muslims. This shows that God cannot bring about genuine faith by force, indicating a limit on power that aligns with wisdom.
4
Examine the objection about leaving
The opponent says that leaving people to their choices is not opposition to will. Maturidi responds that if leaving implies no will, then we have the earlier problem of increasing God's kingdom with what He does not will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to Maturidi, what does the fact that God does not prevent a particular evil without willing it indicate?
A) God must have willed it.
B) God did not have the power to prevent it.
C) God loves the evil.
D) God is indifferent.

Key Takeaway
God's non-prevention of evil does not imply His will; it may reflect the limits of power consistent with free will.

?
When you encounter evil or suffering in your life, do you instinctively assume that God willed it? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider the relationship between God's power and His will, and what does that demand of your trust in Him?
?
If God's non-prevention of evil is not a sign of His approval, then what responsibility do you have when you see injustice? Does this lesson push you to act differently, knowing that God respects free will and so should you?
?
Think of a time you blamed God for something bad that happened. After this lesson, can you still hold that blame? What must change in your heart about how you view God's role in your trials?

The Human King Analogy: A False Comparison
Maturidi refutes analogy to human kings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's two reasons for rejecting the analogy between God and human kings regarding prevention of evil.

Core Idea
Human kings cannot prevent all evil, and they lack authority over others' actions.

The opponent draws an analogy to human kings who sometimes tolerate evil they dislike but cannot prevent. Maturidi refutes this with two clear reasons: human kings lack the power to prevent everything they dislike, and they have no authority over the actions of others (free will). This preserves God's unique sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy and refutation
Find where the opponent compares God to a human king. Then read Maturidi's two rejoinders.
2
Analyze the first reason: inability to prevent
Maturidi says that if a human king could prevent all he dislikes, he would. But since he cannot, the analogy fails.
3
Analyze the second reason: lack of authority
A human king has no ownership or authority over the actions of others; only God does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه إذا أراد لمن علم أن يختار هو عدواته أن يكون منه بأن يكون عدواً لله، فهذا ليزول معنى الضعف ويظهر الغنى عنه وعن فعله، كما قال: إن الله لغني عن العالمين [العنكبوت ٦/٢٩]. على أنه يزعم أن معنى الإرادة أن لا يُغلب، وقد وجد في هذا، فليقل ما شاء، فهو له في الأول جواب. وأما جوابه بالمحبة والرضا فإنه لا يجوز أن يقال: إن الله يحب إبليس ويرضى به وكذلك الخبائث والأقذار، وإن كان أراد كونهم، فمثله فعل الكفر وكذا كل قبائح الصور والجواهر. والله أعلم. وأجاب لما عورض من الزيادة في ملكه ما لا يريده بالرضا والمحبة، وقد بينا التفريق في ذلك بما هو فعله. ثم قال: إذا قدر على المنع فلم يمنع فليس بممنوع. فيقال له: لو قدر وهو لا يريده ليمنع، فدل كونه بلا إرادة أنه لم يقدر. ومما يبين ذلك أنه لو قهرهم على الإسلام لم يكونوا مسلمين، قهرا، يبيّن أنه لم يكن يقدر على ذلك، وذلك حق الغلبة والقهر في الشاهد. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعارض بتركهم، فيقال: ليس في الترك خلاف له في الإرادة، فيلحق ما ذكرنا من الزيادة في ملكه ما لا يريده. وبالله التوفيق. وعارض بمثله في الشاهد، وهو خطأ لوجهين أحدهما أن ملكنا لا يقدر على المنع، وإلا كان يمنع عن كل شيء لم يرده. والثاني أن ذلك ليس في ملكه ولا سلطانه، لما ليس لملك الأرض على أفعال غيره ملك ولا سلطان. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And after, if He willed for the one He knows will choose enmity to be an enemy to God, then this is so that the meaning of weakness disappears and His self-sufficiency from him and from his action appears, as He said: 'Indeed Allah is free from need of the worlds.' Despite his claim that the meaning of will is that one not be overwhelmed, and this has been found in this, so let him say what he wishes, for it is for him the first answer. As for his answer with love and pleasure, it is not permissible to say that Allah loves Iblis and is pleased with him, and likewise vile things and filth, even if He willed their existence. Similar is the act of disbelief, and likewise every ugly form and substance. And Allah knows best. And he answered when it was objected concerning the increase in His kingdom of what He does not will, with pleasure and love, and we have shown the distinction in that by what is His action. Then he said: If he has power to prevent and did not prevent, then it is not prevented. So it is said to him: If he had power while he does not will it, he would prevent, so the fact that it is without will indicates that he did not have power. And among that which clarifies this is that if he compelled them to Islam, they would not be Muslims, by compulsion. It shows that he was not able to do that, and that is the truth of overwhelming and coercion in the witnessed realm. And there is no power except with Allah. And he objected with their leaving, so it is said: There is no opposition to Him in will in leaving, so what we mentioned of increase in His kingdom of what He does not will attaches. And with Allah is success. And he objected with something similar in the witnessed realm, and it is erroneous for two reasons: one, that our king cannot prevent, otherwise he would prevent everything he does not will. Second, that this is not in his possession or authority, because the earthly king has no possession or authority over the actions of others. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about divine will and prevention. Maturidi begins by addressing the claim that God's will for an enemy's enmity is to eliminate any notion of weakness in God. He then refutes the opponent's (Ka'bi's) use of love and pleasure as synonymous with will, stating that one cannot say God loves or is pleased with Iblis or vile things. The opponent then argues that if God had power to prevent an act but did not, then the act is not prevented (i.e., it is willed). Maturidi responds: if God had power and did not will to prevent, He would have prevented; therefore, the absence of prevention without will shows lack of power. He gives the example of compelling people to Islam: if God forced them, they would not be true Muslims, indicating God could not bring about genuine faith by force. Then the opponent objects by citing cases where God leaves people to their own choices. Maturidi replies that leaving is not opposition to will; but if leaving implies no will, then we would have the earlier problem of increasing God's kingdom with what He does not will. Finally, the opponent analogizes to human kings. Maturidi rejects this for two reasons: first, a human king cannot prevent everything he dislikes; second, a human king has no authority over the actions of others. The argument proceeds step by step, using logical reasoning and Quranic principles.

Key Terms
ملك (mulk)
سلطان (sultan)
منع (manʿ)
قدرة (qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is like a human king who cannot prevent all evil, so He doesn't will it.
Correction: Maturidi shows the analogy fails because human kings lack both power and authority, whereas God has both.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the analogy to a human king erroneous? (Choose the best answer)
A) Because a human king can prevent all evil.
B) Because a human king has authority over actions of others.
C) Because a human king cannot prevent all he dislikes and has no authority over others' actions.
D) Because a human king is more powerful than God.

Key Takeaway
The human king analogy fails because kings lack both the power and the authority that God possesses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself excusing your own failures by comparing yourself to others, do you realize that you are using a flawed analogy similar to the one refuted here? What would it mean to instead measure yourself against God's absolute standard?
?
This lesson shows that human kings lack both power and authority over others' actions. In your life, do you ever act as if you have authority over others that you don't actually have? How can you realign your sense of authority with the truth?
?
The opponent in this passage used an analogy to justify evil in the world. Are there any analogies or excuses you use to justify your own shortcomings or sins? What would it take to stop using those analogies and submit to God's perfect authority?

Maturidi's Distinction Between Love/Pleasure and Will/Wishaa
Maturidi distinguishes divine love/pleasure from will/wishaa using objection, scriptural proof, and rational principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi distinguishes divine love and pleasure from will and wishaa using Quranic evidence and rational principles, and why this distinction is important for understanding divine attributes and the problem of evil.

Core Idea
Love is specific to good; will is universal.

Maturidi addresses a theological objection about deficiency and then presents a scriptural proof from the Quran to show that divine love and pleasure are not identical with divine will and wishaa. He further explains the foundational principle: love and anger are responses to human actions, whereas will is not caused by them. This lesson traces the entire argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial objection
Read the opening lines where Maturidi raises an objection: if something's exit from God's knowledge implies ignorance, why does its exit from His will not imply deficiency? Notice the reply that it implies dislike, not deficiency.
This sets up the need for a distinction.
2
Examine the scriptural evidence
Read the Quranic verses cited. Note that God does not love corruption or disbelief, but He wills both guidance and misguidance. This shows love is specific (makhsusa) while will is general (mu'ammama).
Verses from Surah al-Zumar, al-Baqara, and al-An'am.
3
Grasp the general vs. specific distinction
Understand Maturidi's conclusion: love and pleasure are specified to good deeds, while wishaa and will are universal. God does not love evil but He wills it (as part of His overall plan).
4
Learn the foundational principle
Read from 'wa al-aslu fi dhalika' onwards. Love and anger are meanings necessitated by the actions of servants. Wishaa is not caused by human actions; it is an attribute of God that is not dependent on them.
This is the key rational distinction.
5
Note the timing differences
Maturidi contrasts the timing: according to opponents, will precedes the act; according to him, will accompanies the act. Love and pleasure always occur after the act in customary usage.
Further underlines their distinct natures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بما يُعقب خروج الشيء عن علمه جهلاً، لم لا أوجب خروجه عن إرادته نقصانا، وهو عجز؟ فقال: إنما يعقب الكراهة لا النقصان. قيل: كراهة النهي كذا إرادته نقصانا، والغلبة تحدث نقصانا. وفي كتاب الله أيضا دليل الفرق بين المحبة والرضا، وبين الإرادة والمشيئة بقوله: ولا يرضى لعباده الكفر [الزمر، ٧/٣٩]، وقوله: والله لا يحب الفساد [البقرة، ٢٠٥/٢]، وقوله: إن الله يحب التوابين [البقرة، ٢٢٢/٢]، ولا يحب المعتدين [البقرة، ١٩٠/٢]؛ وقال في المشيئة: من يشاء الله يضلله ومن يشأ يجعله على صراط مستقيم [الأنعام، ٣٩/٦]، وغير ذلك مما يوجب تخصيص المحبة والرضا وتعميم المشيئة والإرادة. مع ما يوصف بهما في أفعاله، ولا يوصف بالرضا والمحبة. على أن المشيئة صَرَفَها إلى القوة حتى جعلها بحكم القسر، فلذلك قوتها توجب ذلك. والأصل في ذلك أن المحبة والسخط معنيان يوجبان بفعل العباد؛ وليست المشيئة كذلك، لما ليس في أفعال العباد معنى يوجب المشيئة، إلا أن يُراد بها الرضا أو التمني. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وفي الشاهد قد يفعل الرجل ما لا يرضى به ولا يحبه ومحال حقيقة فعل لا يريده. وكذا معنى الإرادة متقدم عندهم على الفعل، وعندنا معنى يكون معه، ولا وجه لها بعده؛ وأمر الرضا والسخط والمحبة ونحو ذلك يكون من بعد في المتعارف أبدا. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he objected to himself with what follows the exit of a thing from His knowledge as ignorance: why does its exit from His will not necessitate deficiency, which is incapacity? And he said: It follows dislike, not deficiency. It was said: Dislike of prohibition like His will is deficiency, and being overcome produces deficiency. And in the Book of Allah there is also evidence of the distinction between love and pleasure, and between will and wishaa, by His saying: 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief,' and His saying: 'And Allah does not love corruption,' and His saying: 'Indeed Allah loves those who repent,' and 'He does not love the transgressors'; and He said concerning wishaa: 'Whomever Allah wills He misguides, and whomever He wills He places on a straight path,' and other than that which necessitates the specification of love and pleasure and the generalization of wishaa and will. Along with what is described by them in His actions, and He is not described with pleasure and love. For wishaa He turned to power until He made it by the rule of compulsion, therefore its power necessitates that. And the basis of this is that love and anger are two meanings necessitated by the actions of servants; and wishaa is not like that, because there is no meaning in the actions of servants that necessitates wishaa, unless by it is meant pleasure or desire. And there is no power except with Allah. And in the witnessed realm, a man may do what he is not pleased with and does not love, but it is impossible for him to actually do an act he does not will. And likewise the meaning of will is prior to the act according to them, and according to us it is a meaning that occurs with the act, and there is no aspect for it after the act. And the matter of pleasure, anger, love, and the like always occurs after in the customary. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument distinguishing divine love and pleasure from divine will and wishaa (mashee'a). Maturidi presents an internal objection: if something's exit from God's knowledge leads to ignorance (which is impossible), why does its exit from His will not lead to deficiency? The opponent replies that it leads to dislike (karaha), not deficiency. Maturidi counters that dislike of prohibition can itself imply deficiency. Then he pivots to a scriptural proof. He cites several Quranic verses to show that love (hubb) and pleasure (rida) are explicitly withheld from disbelief and corruption, while will (irada) and wishaa are universal in that God wills and wishes both guidance and misguidance. He concludes that love and pleasure are specific (makhsusa) to good deeds, while wishaa and will are general (mu'ammama). Moreover, God's wishaa is associated with compulsion (qasr) and power (quwwa). He then explains the foundational principle: love and anger are caused by the actions of servants—they are responses to what servants do. In contrast, wishaa is not caused by servants' actions; it is an attribute of God that precedes or accompanies acts. In human experience, a person may do what he dislikes but cannot do what he does not will. Also, for the opponents (Mu'tazila?), will precedes the act; for Maturidi, will accompanies the act and does not occur after it. Love, pleasure, and anger always occur after the act in customary usage. Thus, the distinction resolves the problem of evil: God wills all things but only loves and is pleased with good.

Key Terms
محبة (hubb)
رضا (rida)
إرادة (irada)
مشيئة (mashī'a)
كراهة (karāha)
قسر (qasr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God loves all that He wills.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that love and pleasure are not coextensive with will. God wills all things, but He only loves and approves of what is good.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the fundamental difference between divine love/pleasure and divine will/wishaa?
A) Love and pleasure are general, while will is specific.
B) Love and pleasure are caused by the actions of servants, while will is not.
C) Love and pleasure occur before the act, while will occurs after.
D) There is no difference; they are synonyms.

Key Takeaway
God wills all things but only loves and approves of what is good; love and pleasure are responses to human actions, while will is an attribute that is not caused by them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial you believe God willed, do you trust that He loves you despite it? If you struggle, what specific truth from this lesson can you hold onto?
?
Is there any area of your life where you act out of duty but without love, thinking that is enough for God? How does the distinction between will and love challenge you to seek both obedience and heartfelt approval?
?
The passage says a man may do what he dislikes but not what he does not will. Are there commands of Allah that you obey reluctantly, secretly wishing He had willed otherwise? Will you ask Him to transform your dislike into love?

Refuting the Objection from 'God Wills Ease'
Maturidi refutes the objection from verses like 'God wills ease' and explains their meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that God cannot will disbelief because He wills ease, by interpreting 'will' as permission and license rather than decree.

Core Idea
Quranic 'will' can mean permission, not just decree.

This lesson examines an important objection against Maturidi’s doctrine of divine will: if God wills ease and not hardship, how can He will disbelief, the greatest hardship? Maturidi answers by showing that the word 'will' in such verses carries a different meaning. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the relevant Quranic verses
Start by reading the objection based on 'Allah wills ease for you' and similar verses. Understand the opponent's logic that disbelief is hardship, so God cannot will it.
2
Understand the first answer
Maturidi explains that 'will' in these verses means permission (idhn), allowance (ibaha), and license (rukhsa), not God's eternal decree. This removes the contradiction.
3
Examine the second answer
Maturidi adds that even if the verses were about both matters, they would apply only to those who already believe; for disbelievers, if God willed their faith, it would occur. Their disbelief proves He did not will it.
This is a conditional reading.
4
See the supporting evidence
Maturidi cites verses like 'Whomever Allah wills to guide' and 'Allah wills not to assign them a share in the Hereafter' to show that God's will is specific to certain outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج بقوله تعالى: يريد الله بكم اليسر، ونحو ذلك، وقال: ولا يريد بكم العسر، والكفر أعسر العسر. قيل: الإرادة في هذا تخرج على الإذن والإباحة والرخصة، ليس ذلك من أمر الإيمان في شيء، فكذا [عدم] إرادة العسر. وأيضا إنه لو كان على الأمرين فالوجه أن أولئك قوم قد آمنوا فلم يكن لهم في التحقيق غير الذي أراد، فلو كان من الكافر أراد الإيمان لكان لا يكون سواه، كما إذا أراد فيمن أراد الإيمان لم يكن غيره. وعلى هذا قوله تعالى: فمن يرد الله أن يهديه الآية، وأيد ذلك قوله: يريد الله ألا يجعل لهم حظا في الآخرة؛ وبين في المؤمنين: والله يريد الآخرة، دل أن كل من أراد له الإيمان ينبغي. والله أعلم.
Then he argued by His statement: 'Allah wills ease for you,' and similar, and said: 'And He does not will hardship for you,' and disbelief is the greatest hardship. It was said: Will in this is interpreted as permission, allowance, and license; it is not at all about the matter of faith. Likewise is the non-willing of hardship. Also, if it were about both matters, then the correct view is that those people had already believed, so in reality they had nothing other than what He willed; if He had willed faith from the disbeliever, then it would be nothing else, just as if He willed it in one for whom He willed faith, it would be nothing else. And upon this is His statement: 'Whomever Allah wills to guide,' the verse, and that is supported by His statement: 'Allah wills not to assign them a share in the Hereafter'; and He clarified among the believers: 'And Allah wills the Hereafter'; it indicates that everyone for whom He wills faith, it should be. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection based on Quranic verses that seem to say God wills ease and does not will hardship. The opponent (likely Ka'bi) argues that since disbelief is the greatest hardship, God cannot will it. Maturidi answers that the word 'will' (irada) in these verses is used in the sense of permission (idhn), allowance (ibaha), and license (rukhsa), not as a decree of faith. The verses are about religious concessions, not about God's eternal will. Additionally, if the verses were about both belief and disbelief, then they would apply to those who have already believed; for disbelievers, if God willed faith from them, they would indeed have faith, so the fact they do not shows God did not will it. He then cites other verses: 'Whomever Allah wills to guide...' (6:125) and 'Allah wills not to assign them a share in the Hereafter' (3:176) and 'Allah wills the Hereafter' (8:67) to show that will is made specific to certain outcomes. The passage concludes that everyone for whom God wills faith, it necessarily occurs. Thus, the objection is resolved by interpreting the relevant verses correctly.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
إذن (idhn)
إباحة (ibāḥa)
رخصة (rukhṣa)
هداية (hidāya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot will disbelief because He says He does not will hardship.
Correction: Maturidi explains that 'will' in such verses means permission and license, not decree; moreover, if God willed faith from a disbeliever, it would occur.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi interpret the verse 'He does not will hardship for you' in response to the objection that God does not will disbelief?
A) He says disbelief is not hardship.
B) He says 'will' here means permission and license, not decree.
C) He says the verse applies only to believers.
D) He says God actually wills all things including hardship.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that Quranic 'will' can refer to permission and religious concession, not only eternal decree, thereby preserving the doctrine that God wills all things, including disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been tempted to think that because Allah is merciful, He will overlook your sins? How does this lesson's distinction between permission and decree challenge that assumption? This week, identify one area where you have been relying on God's 'ease' without taking responsibility, and commit to a specific action that aligns with His command.
?
Consider a non-Muslim you know. Does the idea that Allah wills disbelief for some people make you feel detached or responsible? Read the supporting verses Maturidi cites (6:125, 3:176, 8:67) and reflect: How should this knowledge shape your du'a and interaction with them? Write down one concrete change you will make in your attitude or behavior.
?

Based on this lesson, reflect: what does it mean that Allah's will includes both ease (as permission) and hardship (as decree)? This week, when a difficulty arises, pause and say, 'This is from Allah's will,' and ask yourself what He might be teaching you.

Quranic Evidence for the Distinction
Maturidi cites Quranic verses to prove the distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic verses to prove that divine love and pleasure are specified to good actions, while will and volition are general.

Core Idea
Love and pleasure are specific; will and volition are universal.

Maturidi begins by citing Quranic verses that show God's love and pleasure are restricted to certain actions, while verses on will and volition apply to all things. This textual evidence establishes a foundational distinction in divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses
Read the Arabic passage containing the verses about love/pleasure and volition. Notice that love and pleasure are mentioned in contexts of approval (e.g., loving repentance), while volition is mentioned in both guidance and misguidance.
The verse about volition (6:39) shows God wills both guidance and misguidance.
2
Identify the conclusion
After citing the verses, Maturidi concludes that this necessitates 'takhsis' (specification) for love/pleasure and 'ta'mim' (generalization) for will/volition. Understand why.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Ask yourself: If God loves repentance but not corruption, does He will both? Yes, according to the volition verse. So love is not the same as will.
This is the key insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغلبة تحدث نقصانا. وفي كتاب الله أيضا دليل الفرق بين المحبة والرضا، وبين الإرادة والمشيئة بقوله : ولا يرضى لعباده الكفر [الزمر، ۷/۳۹] ، وقوله : والله لا يحب الفساد [البقرة ٢٠٥/٢] ، وقوله : إن الله يحب التوابين ﴾ [البقرة، (۲۲۲/۲]، ولا يحب المعتدين [البقرة، ۱۹٠/٢]؛ وقال في المشيئة : من يشاء الله يضلله ومن يشأ يجعله على صراط مستقيم [الأنعام، ٣٩/٦] ، وغير ذلك مما يوجب تخصيص المحبة والرضا [ و ]تعميم المشيئة والإرادة. مع ما يوصف بهما في أفعاله، ولا يوصف بالرضا والمحبة. على أن المشيئة صَرَفَها إلى القوة حتى جعلها بحكم القسر ، فلذلك قوتها توجب ذلك. والأصل في ذلك أن المحبة والسخط معنيان يوجبان بفعل العباد ؛ وليست المشيئة كذلك، لما ليس في أفعال العباد معنى يو- المشيئة ، إلا أن يُراد بها الرضا أو التمني. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وفي الشاهد قد يفعل الرجل ما لا يرضى به ولا يحبه ومحال حقيقة فعل لا يريده. وكذا معنى الإرادة متقدم عندهم على الفعل، وعندنا معنى يكون معه، ولا وجه لها بعده؛ وأمر الرضا والسخط والمحبة ونحو ذلك يكون من بعد في المتعارف أبدا. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And dominance causes deficiency. And in the Book of God there is also evidence for the distinction between love (al-maḥabbah) and pleasure (al-riḍā), and between will (al-irādah) and volition (al-mashī’ah), by His saying: "And He is not pleased (yarḍā) with disbelief for His servants" [39:7], and His saying: "And God does not love (lā yuḥibbu) corruption" [2:205], and His saying: "Indeed God loves (yuḥibbu) the repentant" [2:222], and "He does not love the transgressors" [2:190]; and He said concerning volition: "Whomever God wills (yasha’) He leaves astray, and whomever He wills He places on a straight path" [6:39], and other such verses that necessitate specifying love and pleasure and generalizing volition and will. Along with what is described by them in His actions, and He is not described by pleasure and love. Since volition He directed to power until He made it under the rule of compulsion, therefore its power necessitates that. And the foundation of this is that love and anger are two meanings necessitated by the action of servants; and volition is not like that, because there is nothing in the actions of servants that necessitates volition, unless what is meant by it is pleasure or wish. And there is no power except through God. And in the witnessed world, a man may do something he is not pleased with and does not love, but it is impossible for a real action that he does not will. Likewise, the meaning of will is prior according to them to the action, while according to us it is a meaning that accompanies it, and there is no aspect for it after the action; and the matter of pleasure, anger, love, and the like always occurs afterwards in common usage. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between divine attributes of love/pleasure and will/volition. Maturidi begins by citing Quranic verses that attribute love and pleasure to God specifically for good actions (e.g., loving repentance, not loving corruption), while verses on volition are general, covering both guidance and misguidance. This proves that love and pleasure are specified, while will/volition are universal. He then explains that love and anger are consequences of servants' actions—they respond to what occurs. Volition, however, does not depend on the action; it is inherent in the act itself. He uses a human example: a person can do what he does not love but cannot do what he does not will. Thus, will is intrinsic to the act, while love/pleasure follow the act. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine attributes and avoiding anthropomorphism in attributing reactive emotions to God.

?
