The Purpose of Command and Will
Maturidi explains the distinct purposes of command and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third reason: the functions of command (showing lordship) and will (showing sovereignty) require their affirmation for different domains.

Core Idea
Command shows lordship; will shows sovereignty.

The third reason delves into the purposes behind command and will. Command serves to elevate the commander and demonstrate his lordship over the commanded. Will, on the other hand, asserts choice and the absence of compulsion, affirming God's absolute sovereignty. This lesson explains why both attributes must be affirmed but in different scopes.

Guided Path
1
Read the third reason
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the benefits of command and the right of will.
2
Understand the benefit of command
Command manifests the commander's high status and lordship. Without command, that aspect is hidden.
Command involves subjugation and showing favor.
3
Understand the right of will
Will demonstrates that God is not compelled; denying will would imply He is overpowered.
Thus will must be affirmed for all things to establish His dominion.
4
Synthesize the two
Both command and will are necessary but for different reasons: command for some things, will for everything.
They are not parallel; one cannot be used to judge the other.

?

?

The Story of Ibrahim and the Implication of Bada'
Maturidi uses the Ibrahim story to illustrate command without will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth reason: the story of Ibrahim's sacrifice shows that God's command can occur without His real will, because otherwise it would imply bada' (reconsideration), which is impossible for God.

Core Idea
A command can be given without intending its execution.

The fourth reason uses the well-known story of Prophet Ibrahim. God commanded him to sacrifice his son but then prevented it through a ram. If God had truly willed the sacrifice, preventing it would indicate a change of mind (bada'), which is a sign of ignorance. This demonstrates that the command was not accompanied by a real will for the act. This lesson explores this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth reason
Read the Arabic passage about Ibrahim and the ram.
2
Understand the scenario
God commanded Ibrahim to sacrifice, then ransomed with a ram. The command was real but the act did not happen.
This shows command and will can be separate.
3
Grasp the problem of bada'
If God willed the actual sacrifice, preventing it would mean He changed His will (bada'), which is ignorance. So He did not will the act.
Thus command does not entail will.

And God is the Grantor of success. Also, indeed the benefit of the command is the elevation and highness of the commander, since he subjugates the other and manifests his right and great favors through which he deserves to be his master and worshipped. And the right of will is choice and the negation of compulsion, that he not be overpowered nor prevented from his authority, nor can there be any interference between him and his dominion. And in negating will from Him, this [negation] is [implied]; therefore it is false that He does not will. Likewise, in preventing command and prohibition, therefore it is necessary to affirm command and prohibition regarding both matters to manifest His authority and lordship, and to affirm will in all things to establish His dominion and the inability of creatures to will anything in His dominion and authority. And God is the Grantor of success. Also, indeed God commanded Ibrahim to sacrifice and then ransomed with a ram. It is not permissible that He willed the actual act of sacrifice then prevented it through a substitute, because that is a sign of reconsideration (bada') and a mark of ignorance. So the command was not accompanied by the reality of will. And there is no power except through God. The summary is what we have explained concerning the division of the meanings of will and agreement upon the verification of the meaning that he holds; there is nothing beyond that except a verbal obstacle or a turning from its proper direction to a direction that, from that direction, is considered bad by the opponent. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector raises a question: if commanding sins is bad, why is willing them not also bad? Maturidi gives several reasons (wujuh). The first reason is that there is a contradiction in commanding a sin because the commandment itself would transform the act into obedience, thereby nullifying its sinfulness; but will does not have this effect. An agent wills his own act but cannot command himself. The second reason: God is described as having will over His own acts but cannot have a command over them, so the two are different. Moreover, God willed the destruction of prophets and survival of enemies but did not command these; He commanded us to pray for the opposite. The third reason: the function of command is to show the commander's superiority and lordship, while the function of will is to show choice and absence of compulsion. Denying will would imply God is overpowered, so it is necessary to affirm will for all things. The fourth reason: the story of Ibrahim – God commanded him to sacrifice his son but then ransomed with a ram; if God had truly willed the actual sacrifice, He would not have prevented it, because that would imply reconsideration (bada'), which is ignorance. Thus the command was not accompanied by real will. The summary states that the meanings of will are divided, and the correct meaning is agreed upon; any objection is merely verbal or a misdirection.

Key Terms
أمر (command)
ذبح (sacrifice)
فداء (ransom)
بداء (reconsideration)
جهل (ignorance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God commands something, He must will it to happen.
Correction: The story shows a command without will for the act; God's will is distinct from His command.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say it is impossible that God willed the actual sacrifice?
A) Because Ibrahim disobeyed.
B) Because preventing it would show bada' (reconsideration), which is ignorance.
C) Because God never wills sacrifice.
D) Because the ram was not a substitute.

Key Takeaway
The Ibrahim story demonstrates that God can command an act without willing its occurrence, proving command and will are not identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a command from Allah must indicate His will for that event to occur in reality? How does the story of Ibrahim challenge you to reconsider your understanding of divine commands?
?
If Allah commanded Ibrahim to sacrifice but did not intend the act itself, what does that teach you about the nature of trials? How might you respond when you face a command from Allah that seems impossible to fulfill?
?
In what area of your life are you holding onto a belief that if Allah commands something, He must want it to happen exactly as commanded? How does this lesson liberate or unsettle you?

Summary: The Division of Meanings of Will
Maturidi concludes with a summary about the meanings of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's concluding summary: the meanings of will are divided, and the correct meaning is agreed upon; any objection is merely verbal.

Core Idea
The correct meaning of will is agreed; objections are semantic.

After presenting four reasons, Maturidi summarizes his argument. He states that the meanings of will (irāda) are divided, and the correct interpretation—the one he defends—is agreed upon. Any remaining objection is merely a verbal issue or a misdirection from the proper meaning. This lesson ties together the entire discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the summary
Read the Arabic passage that begins 'Jumlahu...' (The summary is...).
2
Understand the division of meanings
Maturidi mentions that 'will' has different senses; the meaning he holds is the one agreed upon.
Clarifying the meaning resolves the objection.
3
Recognize remaining objections as verbal
Any further objection is due to a verbal misunderstanding or a shift in perspective.
The core argument stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل إذ قبح الأمر بالمعاصي لم لا قبح إرادة كونها قيل لأوجه أحدها التناقض في الأمر وليس ذلك في الإرادة لأن الفعل ربما يصير طاعة بالأمر فمحال الأمر بالمعصية لأنه يصير بالأمر طاعة فيبطل معنى المعصية ليكون بها الأمر وليست الإرادة كذلك ألا يرى أن كل فاعل مريد لفعله ومحال أن يقال أمر نفسه بفعله ثبت أنهما مختلفان ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضاً إن الله يوصف بالإرادة في فعله ومحال أن يكون عليه أمر فيه فثبت أن أحد الوجهين ليس هو دليل الآخر مع ما كان الله تعالى مريدا هلاك الأنبياء والأخيار وبقاء الأعداء والأشرار والسعة لهؤلاء في الدنيا ولم يأمر بذلك بل أمرنا بالدعاء بهلاك هؤلاء وبقاء أولئك والله الموفق. وأيضاً إن فائدة الأمر رفعة الآمر وعلوه حيث استعبد الآخر وأظهر فيه حقه وعظيم مننه التي بها استحق أن يكون سيدا له ومعبودا وحق الإرادة الاختيار ونفي الغلبة أن لا يقهر ولا يمنع عن سلطانه ولا يحال بينه وبين ملكه وفي دفع الإرادة هذا لذلك بطل أن لا يريد وكذلك في المنع عن الأمر والنهي لذلك لزم القول بالأمر والنهي على الأمرين ليظهر سلطانه وربوبيته ولزم الإرادة في الكل ليحق ملكه وعجز الخلق عن أن يريدوا في ملكه وسلطانه والله الموفق. وأيضا إن الله أمر إبراهيم بالذبح والفداء بالكبش فلا يجوز أن يكون أراد فعل حقيقة الذبح ثم يمنع عنه بالبدل لأنه آية البداء وعلامة الجهل فكان الأمر لا بالذي به حقيقة الإرادة ولا قوة إلا بالله. وجملته ما بينا من انقسام معاني الإرادة والاتفاق على تحقيق المعنى الذي يذهب إليه وليس وراء ذلك إلا بمانع في اللفظ أو صرف عن جهته إلى جهة هي من تلك الجهة قبيح عند الخصم ولا قوة إلا بالله.
If someone says: Since commanding sins is reprehensible, why is willing their occurrence not reprehensible? It is said: For several aspects. One of them is the contradiction in the command, and that is not in the will, because the action might become obedience through the command, so it is impossible to command a sin, because through the command it becomes obedience, thus the meaning of sin is nullified, and the command would be for it; but the will is not like that. Do you not see that every agent wills his own action, and it is impossible to say he commands himself to do it? It is established that they are different. And there is no power except through God. Also, indeed God is described with will in His own act, and it is impossible that He should have a command over it. So it is established that one of the two aspects is not an indication of the other, especially since God Almighty wills the destruction of prophets and the righteous, and the survival of enemies and the wicked, and abundance for these in this world, and He did not command that, but rather He commanded us to pray for the destruction of these and the survival of those. And God is the Grantor of success. Also, indeed the benefit of the command is the elevation and highness of the commander, since he subjugates the other and manifests his right and great favors through which he deserves to be his master and worshipped. And the right of will is choice and the negation of compulsion, that he not be overpowered nor prevented from his authority, nor can there be any interference between him and his dominion. And in negating will from Him, this [negation] is [implied]; therefore it is false that He does not will. Likewise, in preventing command and prohibition, therefore it is necessary to affirm command and prohibition regarding both matters to manifest His authority and lordship, and to affirm will in all things to establish His dominion and the inability of creatures to will anything in His dominion and authority. And God is the Grantor of success. Also, indeed God commanded Ibrahim to sacrifice and then ransomed with a ram. It is not permissible that He willed the actual act of sacrifice then prevented it through a substitute, because that is a sign of reconsideration (bada') and a mark of ignorance. So the command was not accompanied by the reality of will. And there is no power except through God. The summary is what we have explained concerning the division of the meanings of will and agreement upon the verification of the meaning that he holds; there is nothing beyond that except a verbal obstacle or a turning from its proper direction to a direction that, from that direction, is considered bad by the opponent. And there is no power except through God.

Refuting Bad' in Divine Will
Maturidi refutes bad' using the Ibrahim example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God's command to Ibrahim does not imply a change in His will, and why bad' would be a sign of ignorance.

Core Idea
God's command does not always reflect His actual will.

This passage uses the story of Ibrahim's sacrifice to refute the idea that God can change His will. Maturidi argues that if God had truly willed the slaughter, He would not have substituted it with a ram. The command was for a test, not a change in will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi builds his argument.
The OCR may have errors, but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the central claim
Find the sentence that states what would be a 'sign of bad' and a mark of ignorance.' Understand why bad' is problematic for divine perfection.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Trace the logic: God commanded Ibrahim to slaughter, then ransomed with a ram. If He had truly willed the slaughter, He would not have prevented it. Thus, the command was not an expression of real will.
This refutes the notion that God changes His mind.
4
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on why this matters for divine attributes: God's will is eternal and unchanging. Bad' would imply ignorance or change, which are impossible for God.
This lesson establishes a key principle in Maturidi kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله أمر إبراهيم بالذبح والفداء بالكبش، فلا يجوز أن يكون أراد فعل حقيقة الذبح ثم يمنع عنه بالبدل، لأنه آية البداء وعلامة الجهل، فكان الأمر لا بالذي به حقيقة الإرادة. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وجملته ما بينا من انقسام معاني الإرادة والاتفاق على تحقيق المعنى الذي يذهب إليه، وليس وراء ذلك إلا بمانع في اللفظ، أو صرف عن جهته إلى جهة هي من تلك الجهة قبيح عند الخصم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.

ثم الأصل الذي يقع عليه الفعل في الشاهد أن يكون على إرادة، أو غلبة، أو سهو. فكل من خرج في شيء عن الوصف بالغلبة فيه والسهو لزم الوصف بالإرادة التي هي لا بها في الحقيقة، فهي أقسام قد بينا ذلك فيما تقدم. والله الموفق.

وأما التي هي على أن القول في الشاهد فيما في الحقيقة إرادة فهي التي يكون بها الفعل لا محالة، فعندنا تكون معه، وعند المعتزلة قبل الفعل بلا فصل. وما عدا ذلك مما قد يكون الفعل إذا وجدت ولا يكون فهي التمني المعروف، والله يجل عن هذا الوصف. ثبت أن إرادته على الوجه الأول، وأنه يتحقق الفعل على الوجه الذي أراد به. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also: Indeed God commanded Ibrahim with the slaughter and the ransom with the ram. It is not permissible that He willed the act of the actual slaughter then prevented it by a substitute, because that would be a sign of bad' (change of will) and a mark of ignorance. So the command was not with that which is the reality of will. And there is no power except with God. The summary of it is what we have explained about the division of the meanings of will and the agreement on the verification of the meaning to which he [the opponent] goes, and there is nothing beyond that except an obstacle in the wording, or a turning from its direction to a direction which, from that direction, is ugly in the view of the opponent. And there is no power except with God.

Then the basis upon which an action is based in the witnessed world is that it be by will, or domination, or forgetfulness. Whoever departs from the description of domination and forgetfulness in something necessarily is described by the will which is [that] by which [the act is] in reality. So they are divisions; we have already explained that in what preceded. And God is the Grantor of success.

As for that which is such that the statement in the witnessed world concerning that which is truly will is that it is that by which the action occurs inevitably. So with us, it occurs with it [the will], and with the Mu'tazila, it is before the action without interval. And other than that, which may be such that the action occurs when it [the will] exists and may not occur, that is the well-known wish (tamanni), and God is exalted above this description. It is established that His will is in the first manner, and that the action is actualized in the manner that He willed it. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is a proof and classification concerning the nature of divine will. The author begins with the example of the command to Ibrahim to sacrifice his son, which was then ransomed with a ram. He argues that if God had truly willed the actual slaughter, He would not have prevented it by a substitute, as that would imply bad' (change of will), a mark of ignorance. Thus, the command was not issued with real will (irada haqiqiyya). The author then summarizes that the meanings of will are divided, and the opponent's view is based on a verbal obstacle or a misdirection that is logically ugly. Next, he presents a classification: every action in the witnessed world arises from one of three bases: will (irada), domination (ghalaba), or forgetfulness (sahw). If an action is not due to domination or forgetfulness, it must be by will, which is the true cause of the action in reality. He then distinguishes true will from wish (tamanni). True will necessitates the action inevitably; with the Maturidis, the will is concomitant with the action, while the Mu'tazila hold that it precedes the action without interval. Wish, on the other hand, may or may not result in the action. God is exalted above having a mere wish. The conclusion is that God's will is of the first type, and the action occurs exactly as He willed it.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
بداء (bad')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's command to Ibrahim means He willed the slaughter in reality.
Correction: The passage shows that the command was for testing, not because God intended the actual act of slaughter.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not permissible that God willed the actual slaughter of Ibrahim's son?
A) Because He later ransomed with a ram, which would indicate a change of will.
B) Because Ibrahim refused to obey.
C) Because slaughtering a son is inherently evil.
D) Because the command was only a test from the beginning.

Key Takeaway
God's command to Ibrahim was not an expression of real will, and bad' (change of will) is incompatible with divine perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a divine command that seems impossible or is not fulfilled in the way you expect, do you trust that God's ultimate will is good even if the command was not literal? Or do you doubt His wisdom?
?
The story of Ibrahim shows that obedience is required even when the apparent outcome contradicts what we think God's will is. Where in your life are you holding back from obedience because you cannot see the full picture?
?
If God's will is unchanging, how does that affect your response to trials? Do you accept that even when a command is not fulfilled literally, it is still a test of your faith?

Classification of Actions and True Will
Maturidi classifies actions and distinguishes true will from wish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three bases of actions in the witnessed world and the distinction between true will and mere wish.

Core Idea
True will inevitably brings about its action; wish does not.

Maturidi classifies actions into three categories: will, domination, and forgetfulness. He then distinguishes true will from wish, noting that God's will is of the first kind. This lesson clarifies the mechanism of divine will and its relationship to action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the classification and the distinction between will and wish.
The passage contains a point of disagreement with the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the three bases
Find the sentence that lists the three bases: will, domination, forgetfulness. Understand the rule: if an action is not from domination or forgetfulness, it is from will.
3
Distinguish will from wish
Note the contrast: true will necessitates the action inevitably (with the act for Maturidis; before the act for Mu'tazila). Wish may or may not result in the action.
God is exalted above wish.
4
Apply to divine will
Conclude that God's will is true will, meaning that whatever He wills must occur exactly as He wills it.
This upholds divine power and wisdom.

A) True will is always before the action; wish is after.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'inshaAllah' about a plan, do you truly intend it with a will that drives action, or is it sometimes a wish that you hope might happen without real commitment? How does that compare to God's will, which always brings about what He intends?
?
Think of a time you acted out of forgetfulness or under compulsion. Does that reveal something about the limits of your own will? Now consider that God's will is never subject to forgetfulness or domination—what does that demand of your reliance on Him?
?
If you truly believed that God's will is always effective and never a mere wish, would you pray differently? Would you ask with more certainty? What keeps you from that level of trust?

Attributing Good Actions to God: Thankfulness, Not Just Command
Maturidi states agreement with Mu'tazila on avoiding evil attributions, then discusses attribution of good actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Maturidi and the Mu'tazila agree on avoiding evil attributions to God, and why Maturidi favors the interpretation of thankfulness and praise over command and empowerment when attributing good actions to God.

Core Idea
Good actions belong to God by thankfulness, not just command.

Imam al-Maturidi begins by establishing a crucial methodological principle shared with the Mu'tazila: nothing that implies ugliness should be attributed to God. He then applies this to the specific question of how we attribute good actions to Him. The Mu'tazila focus on God's command and empowerment, but Maturidi argues that the deeper meaning is thankfulness and praise, which distinguishes believers from disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Try to grasp the overall flow before diving into analysis.
2
Identify the general agreement
Notice the opening statement: Maturidi and Mu'tazila agree that God should not be attributed with anything that implies ugliness in names. This is a shared principle.
Ugliness in names refers to terms that imply imperfection or evil.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila say good actions are attributed to God because He commanded them, called to them, and gave strength for them. This is one valid form of attribution.
4
Understand Maturidi's preferred interpretation
Maturidi says the primary meaning when attributing good actions to God is thankfulness and praise (shukr wa ḥamd). He does not deny the other interpretation, but considers this more appropriate because it distinguishes believers.
Command and empowerment are shared by believers and disbelievers, but thankfulness is unique to believers.
5
Reflect on the example of faith
The passage gives the example of faith as a blessing from God. Reflect on how this illustrates the thankfulness interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نتفق والمعتزلة على أن الله تعالى لا يضاف إليه شيء من الخلق أو الأفعال إلا من الوجه الذي لا يوهم القبح في الأسماء، وما يوهم ذلك فحقه أن ينفى عنه ذلك. ويخرج على هذا مسائل. إحداها في وجه إضافة ما أضيف إلى الله من الخيرات أنها من الله. قالت المعتزلة تضاف إليه من حيث أمر بها ودعا إليها وقوي عليها. وقلنا نحن هذا من الإضافة وإن كان حسنا فلا هذا يراد بالإضافة إليه عند ذكر الأفعال، ولكن المراد الشكر والحمد له إذا ذكرت الأفعال. وقد يجوز الأول، وهذا أولى، لأنه من حيث الأمر والدعاء والتقوية اشترك فيه المؤمن والكافر، ومن جهة الشكر والحمد يختلف. ومما يبين ذلك جواز القول المطلق إن الإيمان من نعم الله ومننه، وإن المؤمن قد أنعم الله عليه ومن.
Then we agree with the Mu'tazila that God, Most High, is not attributed with anything of creation or actions except from the aspect that does not imply ugliness in names; and what implies that it is right to negate that from Him. And derived from this are issues. The first of them is concerning the aspect of attributing what is attributed to God of good things that they are from God. The Mu'tazila said: They are attributed to Him from the aspect that He commanded them, called to them, and gave strength for them. And we said: This is [a type of] attribution, but even if it is good, this is not what is meant by attribution to Him when mentioning actions; rather the meaning is thankfulness and praise to Him when actions are mentioned. And the first is possible, but this is more appropriate, because in terms of command, call, and empowerment, the believer and the disbeliever share, whereas in terms of thankfulness and praise they differ. And among what clarifies that is the permissibility of the absolute statement that faith is from God's blessings and favors, and that the believer has been blessed and favored by God.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage addresses a methodological issue: how to attribute good actions to God without implying evil. Imam al-Maturidi states that both he and the Mu'tazila agree that God is not attributed with anything that implies ugliness (qubh) in names. If an attribution would imply ugliness, it must be negated. The first derived issue (mas'ala) is about the attribution of good things (khayrāt) to God. The Mu'tazila say such attribution is because God commanded, called, and empowered them. Maturidi responds that while that is a valid form of attribution, the primary intended attribution when mentioning actions is one of thankfulness and praise (shukr wa ḥamd) to God. This is more appropriate because command and empowerment are shared by believers and disbelievers (both can do good acts when commanded), but thankfulness is exclusive to believers. Thus, saying 'faith is from God' is a blessing and favor that distinguishes believers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi denies that God commands good acts.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly says 'the first is possible' (i.e., the command interpretation is valid), but he prefers the thankfulness interpretation as more appropriate.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam al-Maturidi consider the 'thankfulness and praise' interpretation of attribution to God as more appropriate?
A) Because it avoids implying ugliness
B) Because command and empowerment are shared by believers and disbelievers
C) Because it is the only valid interpretation
D) Because the Mu'tazila rejected the command interpretation

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Maturidi holds that attributing good actions to God primarily means thanking and praising Him, since command and empowerment are shared by all, but gratitude distinguishes believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'All praise is due to Allah' after a good deed, do you truly feel an acute awareness that this action is a gift from Him, or has it become a routine phrase? How would your heart change if you genuinely tasted thankfulness for every good act?
?
Think of a good deed you did recently. Did you attribute it to God merely as a command you fulfilled, or did you sense a personal favor from Him to you? What would it mean to live every good action as a unique blessing from God to you, distinguishing you from someone who simply obeys without gratitude?
?
The lesson says that command, call, and empowerment are shared by believer and disbeliever. Does this reality unsettle you? How does it challenge your assumption that your obedience makes you superior? Will you now investigate whether your good deeds arise from mere compliance or from a heart overflowing with thanks to the One who singled you out for faith?

Actions Attributed to God: From Gratitude, Not Command
Maturidi argues actions are attributed from gratitude and refutes al-Ka'bi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why actions are attributed to God from the perspective of praise and gratitude rather than command, and how this refutes al-Ka'bi's position.

Core Idea
Divine attribution is about gratitude, not obligation.

This passage from Imam al-Maturidi tackles a fundamental question: on what basis do we attribute actions to God? He argues that command (amr) implies mere obligation and burden, which is not a fitting basis for attribution to the All-Generous. Instead, actions are attributed to God only from the perspective of divine praise and gratitude (hamd wa shukr). This principle is then used to refute the views of al-Ka'bi, who attempted to limit divine attribution to only 'good' acts and claimed obedience is attributed by command.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the overall argument. Note the flow from principle to examples to refutation.
2
Identify the key principle
Locate the statement that actions are attributed from the perspective of gratitude, not command. Note how the author uses Quranic verses to support this.
The principle is stated after the examples.
3
Examine the refutation of al-Ka'bi
Read al-Ka'bi's claim and the author's critique. Understand why al-Ka'bi's view is flawed: he limits attribution to 'good' acts but then says obedience is by command, which is inconsistent.
The author questions 'what good is in that?'
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The faqih concludes that evils are not attributed to God because there is no aspect of gratitude. Consider how this aligns with the overall principle.
5
Consider the implications
Think about how this principle shapes our understanding of divine justice and human responsibility. Why does it matter that attribution is based on gratitude?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنه لولا فضل الله عليه ما زُكِّي، ولَمَسّه عذاب عظيم. ومن هذا الوجه لا يضاف إلى الله في الكافر، وإذا لم تذكر الأفعال فعلى الأمر. والله الموفق. ولهذا طعن الله قال بالكتاب المبدل أنه من عند الله وبإضافتهم البحيرة إليه من ونحوها؛ إنهم ادعوا الأمر بذلك، فبرأ الله نفسه عن ذلك، وأخبر أن ذلك من عمل الشيطان، وأنهم قالوا ذلك حسدًا من عند أنفسهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ولا يجوز أيضاً الفعل من حيث الأمر، لأنه ليس فيه إلا إلزام، وفي ذلك مؤن عظيمة لا يضاف إليه بذلك، بل من جهة الحمد والشكر كما قال: بل الله يمن عليكم، الآية، وقال: فلولا فضل الله عليكم ورحمته، الآية. وقال الكعبي: لا يضاف إلى الله إلا الحسن الجميل، ثم زعم في إضافة الطاعات إليه أنه من وجه الأمر. وأي حسن في ذلك؟ وقد بيّنا ما يدخل على ذلك. وزعم أنه لا تضاف إليه الشرور، لأنه نهى عنها، ولا تضاف إليه. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: وكذلك عندنا لا تضاف إليه لما بينا أن وجه الإضافة للشكر، ولا وجه في ذلك.
And that if not for God's grace upon him, he would not be purified, and a great punishment would touch him. From this aspect, [the action] is not attributed to God in the disbeliever, and if actions are not mentioned, then [they are attributed] by command. And God is the giver of success. And for this reason, God criticized those who said about the altered Book that it is from God, and their attribution of the bahirah to Him and the like; indeed they claimed command in that, so God declared Himself innocent of that, and informed that that is from the work of Satan, and that they said that out of envy from themselves. And there is no power except in God. Also, action is not permissible from the perspective of command, because there is nothing in it except obligation, and in that are great burdens that are not attributed to Him by that, but from the perspective of praise and gratitude, as He said: 'Rather, God bestows favor upon you,' the verse, and He said: 'If not for God's grace upon you and His mercy,' the verse. And al-Ka'bi said: Nothing is attributed to God except the good and beautiful. Then he claimed that in the attribution of acts of obedience to Him, it is from the perspective of command. What good is in that? And we have shown what enters upon that. And he claimed that evils are not attributed to Him because He forbade them, and they are not attributed to Him. The faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: Likewise, according to us, they are not attributed to Him because we have shown that the aspect of attribution is for gratitude, and there is no aspect in that.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the proper basis for attributing actions to God. The author begins by grounding the discussion in a Quranic principle: without God's grace, no one is purified. From this perspective, actions of disbelievers are not attributed to God. He then criticizes those who falsely attribute altered scriptures or pagan practices (like bahirah) to God's command; God declares Himself innocent of such attributions. The author then states the general rule: action is not attributed to God from the perspective of command because command entails mere obligation and burden, but rather from the perspective of praise and gratitude (hamd wa shukr), citing Quranic verses. He then refutes al-Ka'bi's view that only good and beautiful acts are attributed to God, and that acts of obedience are attributed by command. The author questions what 'good' is in that attribution, implying it is not genuinely praiseworthy. He notes al-Ka'bi's claim that evils are not attributed because God forbade them. The faqih concludes by affirming the author's position: attribution is based solely on gratitude, not command.

وجه الأمر
وجه الحمد والشكر
الكعبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God commands an action, it should be attributed to Him because He commanded it.
Correction: Command implies mere obligation and burden, not praiseworthiness. Attribution to God is based on gratitude for His favors, not on His command.

Checkpoint

B) Gratitude (hamd wa shukr)
C) Creation (khalq)
D) Necessity (darura)

Key Takeaway
Attributing actions to God is based on gratitude for His favors, not on His mere command, because command only conveys obligation, not praiseworthiness.

?
When you perform an act of obedience—prayer, charity, fasting—do you feel you are fulfilling a command, or responding to a gift? Which feeling makes your heart more grateful? What would change if you truly believed every good deed is a response to His grace?
?
The author says command implies burden. Do you find your religious duties burdensome? If so, how might shifting your view from 'command' to 'gratitude' transform your experience of worship?
?
Al-Ka'bi thought only 'good and beautiful' acts could be attributed to God. Do you subconsciously believe that God is only responsible for the good in your life, while the evil is purely from yourself? How does the lesson's emphasis on universal grace challenge that assumption? What would it mean for your relationship with God if you saw even your failures as occasions for His mercy?

The Foundation of Divine Action: Grace and Justice
Maturidi states the principle that divine action is creation as fitting, always grace or justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi foundational principle that all of Allah's actions are creations of everything as most fitting, either as grace or justice, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Allah's every act is creation as fitting, either grace or justice.

Imam Maturidi establishes the axiom that governs all discussions of divine action. He defines the meaning of Allah's action as creation, and then clarifies that this creation always takes one of two forms: grace or justice. Once this principle is understood, the objections of the Mu'tazila about divine misguidance and similar issues dissolve. Let us read his precise formulation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'متقدماً في فعله أو عادلاً'.
2
Identify the two aspects of divine action
The passage explicitly states that Allah's action is described as either benevolent (tafaddul) or just (adl). Underline these two terms and note their importance.
These two cover all divine actions.
3
Understand the refutation of Mu'tazila
The author argues that if this principle is applied to problematic verses (like misguidance and sealing of hearts), the Mu'tazila deceptions collapse. Reflect on how the principle resolves apparent conflicts.
The key is that all actions are creations of what is most fitting.
4
Reflect on the consequence
The passage concludes that whatever way Allah's action is ascribed, it is realized as creation. Ask yourself: How does this principle guarantee that Allah's actions are always good?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل في هذا كله عندنا أن الله إذ هو موصوف بفعله؛ ومعنى فعله خلقه كل شيء على ما هو أولى به، متفضلاً في فعله أو عادلاً؛ لا يخلو وصف فعله عن هذين، وحقيقته الأول، فصار بأي وجه أضيف إليه من طريقي فعله محققاً له معنى خلق. ولو ذكر ذا في الإضلال وما ذكر في الطبع وغيره لم يُحتمل شيء من تمويهات المعتزلة؛ فكذلك إذ ذلك معنى فعله. والله الموفق.
And the foundation in all of this, according to us, is that Allah, since He is described by His action, and the meaning of His action is His creation of everything as is most fitting (awla) for it, being benevolent (mutafaddilan) in His action or just (ˈadilan); His action's description does not lack these two, and its reality is the first [mentioned: what is most fitting]. So it becomes that in whatever way it is ascribed to Him from the two aspects of His action, it is realized that He has the meaning of creation. And if this were mentioned regarding misguidance and what was mentioned regarding sealing [of hearts] and other things, nothing of the deceptions (tamwihan) of the Mu'tazila would be entertained; so likewise here, since that is the meaning of His action. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a conclusive statement of the Maturidi principle regarding divine action. The author asserts that the foundation (asl) in all the previous discussions about ascription of actions to Allah is that Allah is described by His action (fi'l). The meaning of 'action' here is His creation (khalq) of everything in the way that is most fitting (awla) for it, and this creation is either an act of grace (tafaddul) or justice (ˈadl). The description of Allah's action is never devoid of these two aspects—grace and justice—and the reality of His action is the first (i.e., what is most fitting). Consequently, no matter how an act is ascribed to Allah, once it is realized as coming from the two ways of His action (i.e., grace or justice), it is established that He has the attribute of creation. The author then argues that if one applies this principle to the question of divine misguidance and similar issues (such as the sealing of hearts mentioned in the Quran), the deceptions (tamwihan) of the Mu'tazila would not hold. Thus, the principle resolves the confusion: all of Allah's actions, including what is called misguidance, are either gracious or just and are creations in the most fitting manner.

Key Terms

خلق
أولى
متفضل
عادل
طريقا فعله
تمويهات المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah's actions can be unjust or arbitrary.
Correction: The passage shows that every action of Allah is either an act of grace or justice, and is creation of what is most fitting, thus always just and good.

Checkpoint

A) Creation and command
B) Grace and Justice
C) Power and will
D) Good and evil

Key Takeaway
Allah's every action is a creation of what is most fitting, and it is either an act of grace or justice, never arbitrary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that feels unfair, do you truly believe that Allah's action towards you is either grace or justice, never arbitrary? Examine your heart: is there any hidden resentment? How can you align your conviction with this foundational principle today?
?

?
The Mu'tazila were deceived because they measured divine justice by human standards. Are you, like them, secretly measuring Allah's actions by your own scale? What would change if you truly lived as though His every act is perfectly fitting?

Refuting the Qadariyya: Misguidance as Divine Act
Maturidi refutes Qadariyya's naming argument and states the principle of divine acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the Qadariyya's claim that misguidance is merely a name and how he grounds it in the principle of divine acts.

Core Idea
Misguidance is not just a name—it is a real act of God as Creator.

The Qadariyya sought to avoid attributing misguidance to God by calling it just a 'name.' Maturidi exposes this as hollow: if it were just a name, why is it only attributed to God? He then lays down the foundational principle that God's act is His creation of everything as either grace or justice, which decisively refutes the Mu'tazili position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Qadariyya claim and Maturidi's initial rebuttal
Read the opening lines where Maturidi states the Qadariyya argument (misguidance by naming) and his two reasons for rejecting it: misguidance is found from others but not attributed to them, and naming lacks superior wisdom.
Notice that he contrasts the Quranic context of power.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence and the principle of divine acts
Read the Quranic verse (6:39) and Maturidi's summary of the principle: God is described by His act, which is creating everything as is fitting, either as grace or justice.
This principle is the key to refuting all Mu'tazili deceptions.
3
See how this principle nullifies Mu'tazili deceptions
Reflect on Maturidi's concluding statement: if this principle were understood regarding misguidance and sealing, none of the Mu'tazila's sophistries would hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا في الإضلال بالتسمية، وذلك فاسد؛ لما وجد من غيره ولم يضف إليه، ولما ليس في التسمية فضل حكمة يذكر في موضع الوصف بالغنى والسلطان كقوله تعالى: (ومن يشأ الله يضلله ومن يشأ يجعله على صراط مستقيم) وذلك في موضع القوة والسلطان. وبالله نستعين. والأصل في هذا كله عندنا أن الله إذ هو موصوف بفعله؛ ومعنى فعله خلقه كل شيء على ما هو أولى به، متفضلاً في فعله أو عادلاً؛ لا يخلو وصف فعله عن هذين، وحقيقته الأول، فصار بأي وجه أضيف إليه من طريقي فعله محققاً له معنى خلق. ولو ذكر ذا في الإضلال وما ذكر في الطبع وغيره لم يُحتمل شيء من تمويهات المعتزلة؛ فكذلك إذ ذلك معنى فعله. والله الموفق.
And they said concerning misguidance that it is by naming, and that is corrupt; because it is found from other than Him and is not attributed to Him, and because there is no superior wisdom in naming that would be mentioned in the context of describing independence and sovereignty, like His statement (and whomever God wills He leads astray, and whomever He wills He places upon a straight path) and that is in the context of power and sovereignty. And with God we seek help. The principle in all this with us is that God is described by His act; and the meaning of His act is His creation of everything as is most appropriate to it, being gracious in His act or just; the description of His act is not free from these two, and its reality is the first. So in whatever manner it is attributed to Him from the two ways of His act, it is verified for Him the meaning of creation. And if this were mentioned concerning misguidance and what was mentioned concerning sealing and other things, none of the deceptions of the Mu'tazila would be plausible; thus here too, since that is the meaning of His act. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Qadariyya's attempt to evade attributing misguidance to God by claiming that such terms are merely 'names' (tasmia) without real effect. Maturidi first declares this argument corrupt: for one, misguidance is found from other sources (like Satan) and is not attributed to those sources, yet they attribute it to God. Furthermore, naming is not a sign of wise purpose that would be appropriate for descriptions of God's self-sufficiency and sovereignty; rather, when the Quran speaks of God leading astray, it is in a context of power and authority (e.g., verse 6:39). Then Maturidi states the fundamental principle (al-asl) in this matter: God is described by His act (fi'l). The meaning of His act is that He creates everything as is most fitting, either as a grace (tafaddul) or as justice ('adl). No divine act is devoid of these two categories. Therefore, however an act is attributed to God—whether as grace or justice—it is verified that He is the Creator (khalq) of that act. If the Mu'tazila had understood this principle regarding misguidance, sealing of hearts, etc., their sophistries would not hold. Thus, attributing misguidance to God is consistent with His being the Creator.

Key Terms
الإضلال
التسمية
الفعل
الخلق
التفضل
العدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Misguidance is merely a name or label applied to God, not a real act of creation.
Correction: Maturidi argues that misguidance is a real divine act; God creates all things, including misguidance, as either grace or justice.

Checkpoint
What is the main principle Maturidi states to refute the Qadariyya claim about misguidance?
A) God's acts are either gracious or just, and everything He creates falls into these categories.
B) God cannot be described as leading astray because it is unjust.
C) Misguidance is actually from Satan, not from God.
D) The term 'misguidance' is a metaphorical name without real meaning.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Qadariyya by showing that misguidance is a real act of God, who creates everything as either grace or justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience spiritual darkness or misguidance in your own life, do you truly believe that God is the one who leads astray—not as a mere name but as a real act? How does that belief change your attitude toward repentance and seeking guidance?
?
Maturidi says every divine act is either grace or justice. Think of a difficult situation you faced that felt like misguidance. Can you see it now as an act of justice? What does that demand from you in terms of gratitude or humility?
?
If misguidance is a real creation of God, how does that shape your view of those who have not been guided? Do you see them as objects of divine justice rather than simply 'lost'? How should that compel you to act toward them today?

The Necessity of Divine Action for Messengers and Scriptures
Maturidi argues that rejecting divine action voids messengers and scriptures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that human actions cannot be independent of Allah, as that would invalidate the purpose of messengers and scriptures.

Core Idea
If actions are not from Allah, messengers and scriptures lose their meaning.

Imam Maturidi presents a devastating logical consequence of denying that all actions originate from Allah. He shows that such a denial unravels the entire framework of divine guidance, making messengers and scriptures pointless. This passage uses a reductio ad absurdum to affirm Allah’s exclusive role as the Creator of all actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the strong concluding phrase 'جل الله وتعالى عن' that emphasizes divine transcendence.
2
Identify the central claim and its consequences
Identify the premise: if actions are not from Allah, then the realities underlying truth and pleasure cannot be traced back to Him. Then trace the three consequences that Maturidi lists: invalidation of messengers/scriptures, invalidation of creation's wisdom (leading to futility), and the characterization of deniers as devils.
Each consequence is introduced by 'يبطل' (becomes invalid) or 'يحصل' (results in).
3
Understand the comparison to the Mu'tazila
Maturidi ends by comparing those who deny divine action to the Mu'tazila claim that Allah was not knowing or powerful at first. This analogy reinforces the absurdity of the position.
This shows that denying divine action leads to anthropomorphic and temporal attributes in Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الحقيقة واللذة وتكون حقيقتهما راجعة إلى الله ، جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى. بل يبطل معنى الرسل والكتب لما هي في التحصيل تصير إلى الله بالأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد، لا منه إلى ذلك . ذلك غيره . ثم يبطل حكمة خلق الخلق ويحصل على العبث [ب] أن كان العلم يبلغ معرفته . ومن يكون خروج فعله على كفران وجحود المنن والكذب في الإخبار، والسفه في الأفعال فهو حقيق أن يكون شيطانا رجيما، فهو كذلك لا ريب فيه . وهو شبيه بقولهم : كان الله غير عالم ولا قادر، ثم صار كذلك ؛ فلعل تدبيره الأفعال التي كانت فيما نسب إلى الخلق [ قد وجد] في ذلك الوقت ، جل الله وتعالى عن
In truth and pleasure, and their reality returns to Allah—exalted is Allah above that and high. Rather, the meaning of messengers and scriptures becomes invalid because in attainment they return to Allah through command, prohibition, promise, and threat, not from Him to that. That is other than Him. Then the wisdom of creating creation becomes invalid and results in futility if knowledge attains [such] knowledge. And whoever's action comes out as ingratitude, denial of blessings, lying in reports, and foolishness in actions, he is worthy to be a cursed devil, so he is without doubt. And this is similar to their saying: Allah was not knowing nor powerful, then He became so; perhaps His management of actions that were attributed to creation [existed] at that time—exalted is Allah above that.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a theological proof by Imam Maturidi demonstrating that attributing human actions to anyone other than Allah leads to absurd consequences. He begins by stating that the reality of things like truth and pleasure ultimately goes back to Allah. He then argues that if actions are not from Allah, the entire purpose of messengers and scriptures becomes nullified, because they revolve around Allah's commands, prohibitions, promises, and threats—not from Him to something else. Furthermore, the wisdom of creation would be invalidated, resulting in futility. He concludes that any being whose actions are characterized by ingratitude, lies, and foolishness is truly a devil. He likens this to the Mu'tazila claim that Allah was not knowing or powerful at one point and then became so, a clear anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqa): reality
الرسل (al-rusul): messengers
الكتب (al-kutub): scriptures
العبث (al-‘abath): futility
الشيطان الرجيم (al-shayṭān al-rajīm): the accursed devil

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi denies any human role in actions; this passage is often misinterpreted as fatalism.
Correction: Maturidi only denies that actions are independent of Allah; he does not negate human acquisition (kasb) or responsibility. Here he focuses on the invalidation of divine guidance if actions were truly independent.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, what becomes invalid if actions are not from Allah?
A) The concept of pleasure
B) The meaning of messengers and scriptures
C) The existence of Allah
D) Human free will

Key Takeaway
All actions, including human deeds, are ultimately from Allah; otherwise, the entire purpose of divine revelation becomes futile.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action, including your own choices, is from Allah? How does this affect your view of your own mistakes? Are you willing to take responsibility while acknowledging Allah's ultimate control?
?
If you didn't believe actions are from Allah, would the commands of the Quran lose their weight for you? Think about a specific command you struggle with—does seeing it as directly from Allah change your attitude?
?
The passage says that attributing actions to other than Allah makes one like a cursed devil. Search your heart: in what ways do you subtly attribute your successes to your own efforts alone? What would change if you constantly saw Allah's hand?

Forgiveness Without Repentance: A Maturidi Argument
Maturidi refutes two views using verse 4:48 to argue that forgiveness of major sins is possible without repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses verse 4:48 to argue that forgiveness of major sins is possible without repentance, and how this refutes two opposing views.

Core Idea
Forgiveness of major sins is possible by Allah's will, not contingent on repentance alone.

This lesson examines a key argument by Imam al-Maturidi regarding the forgiveness of major sins. He focuses on the well-known verse 4:48, which states that Allah does not forgive shirk but forgives what is less than that to whom He wills. Maturidi shows that this verse does not exclude forgiveness for major sins without repentance, contrary to some sectarian interpretations. By understanding his reasoning, we gain insight into the Maturidi position on divine forgiveness and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by carefully reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the key terms: شرك, غفر, مشيئة.
2
Understand the verse and its implication
Maturidi starts by citing the verse. He notes that shirk can be forgiven through repentance, contradicting the literal reading that it is never forgiven. This shows the verse must be interpreted contextually.
3
Identify the two refuted positions
The passage refutes two extreme views: (1) that the verse divides sins into unforgivable shirk and forgivable lesser sins, and (2) that major sins are never forgiven without repentance. Maturidi refutes both by invoking Allah's will to forgive.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that forgiveness is up to Allah's will, in accordance with wisdom. Denying this possibility is foolish. The verse affirms that Allah may forgive major sins without repentance if He wills.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الله تعالى قال: إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به الآية، ومعلوم أن الشرك قد يغفر بالتوبة. فبطل به قول من يجعلها لما قسم الكتاب، وبطل قول من يبطل المغفرة في الكبائر بلا توبة، لأن الله جل ثناؤه جعل لنفسه مشيئة المغفرة، وذلك قد وجد فيما كان في الحكمة؛ فدفعه سفه، جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى؛ فلزم الذي ذكرت في القولين جميعا.
Then Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' the verse, and it is known that shirk is forgiven through repentance. So by this is refuted the saying of one who makes it [the verse, to mean that] what is divided in the Book [is unforgivable], and refuted is the saying of one who invalidates forgiveness of major sins without repentance, because Allah, great is His praise, has made for Himself the will to forgive, and that is found in what is [in accordance with] wisdom; to reject it is foolishness – Allah is exalted above that. So what I mentioned concerning both sayings becomes necessary.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage examines the famous verse from Surah an-Nisa (4:48): 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, but He forgives what is less than that to whom He wills.' Maturidi points out that even shirk (associating partners with Allah) can be forgiven if the person repents – as is known from other verses. Therefore, the verse does not mean that shirk is absolutely unforgivable; it means that without repentance, it is not forgiven. The verse also shows that for lesser sins, forgiveness is up to Allah's will. This refutes two false positions: first, the view that the verse divides sins into two categories – shirk which is never forgiven, and lesser sins which are always forgiven (the Mu'tazila view that major sins require punishment); second, the view that denies the possibility of forgiveness for major sins without repentance (the Khawarij view that the sinner becomes a disbeliever and is not forgiven). Rather, Allah has reserved for Himself the discretion to forgive, and to deny that is to accuse Him of doing something unbecoming of wisdom. Thus Maturidi concludes that forgiveness is possible even for major sins without repentance, as per Allah's will.

Key Terms
شرك
غفر
مشيئة
كبائر
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse excludes all sinners from forgiveness, but it actually leaves forgiveness to Allah's will for sins other than shirk.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the verse does not absolutely deny forgiveness for major sins; it only states that shirk without repentance is unforgivable. For lesser sins, forgiveness is at Allah's discretion.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue about the forgiveness of shirk?
A) It is never forgiven, as the verse says
B) It is forgiven through repentance, as known from other texts
C) Only minor sins are forgiven
D) Shirk is automatically forgiven without repentance

Key Takeaway
Verse 4:48 shows that forgiveness of major sins is subject to Allah's will, not absolutely denied without repentance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin that is not shirk, do you secretly believe that Allah cannot forgive you until you have repented perfectly? Or do you trust that His will to forgive is not tied to the completeness of your repentance? How would your heart change if you truly believed that forgiveness is subject to His will, not your performance? What sin do you need to entrust to His mercy right now, regardless of your readiness?
?
Maturidi calls it 'foolishness' to deny that Allah can forgive without repentance. Reflect: In what area of your life have you implicitly limited Allah's forgiveness—perhaps to sins you deem 'small enough' or after you have done enough good deeds? What would it mean for you to live as if Allah's wisdom and will are the only boundaries on forgiveness, not your own sense of worthiness?
?
The verse 4:48 leaves forgiveness to Allah's will. Yet many Muslims live in either despair (thinking they are beyond forgiveness) or presumption (thinking they are automatically forgiven). Which of these two extremes do you lean toward, and why? How can you cultivate a balanced trust in Allah's will to forgive without neglecting your responsibility to repent?

Distinction Between Maghfura and Takfir with Quranic Evidence
Maturidi defines maghfura and takfir with Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinct meanings of maghfura (forgiveness as concealment) and takfir (expiation through good deeds) as defined by Imam Maturidi, supported by Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Forgiveness conceals sin; expiation removes it through good deeds.

Imam Maturidi begins by clarifying two key terms: maghfura, which means the sin is covered and remains until its eventual removal, and takfir, which means the sin is erased by a subsequent good deed. He supports the latter with clear Quranic evidence. This lesson establishes this foundational distinction that shapes Maturidi's understanding of divine forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, paying attention to the definitions of maghfura and takfir.
Notice that the Mu'tazila are mentioned as holding a different view.
2
Identify the Quranic proofs
List the five Quranic verses cited and reflect on how each supports the concept of expiation.
3
Contrast with the Mu'tazilite view
Consider why the Mu'tazila would reject the idea of expiation and only accept forgiveness as concealment.
This sets up the next lesson's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هي تقع من مجتنب الكبائر مغفورة، وفي هذا إثباتها، ثم التكفير، وهم يجعلونها مغفورة لا مكفّرة، إذ المغفورة هي التي تستر عليها، وفي بقائها إلى مدة دفعها. والمكفّرة هي التي يأتي من صاحبها فعل حسن يكفّر به نحو قوله: فأولئك يبدل الله سيئاتهم حسنات، وقوله: وهل أدلكم على تجارة الآية، وقوله: إن تبدوا الصدقات إلى ذكر التكفير، وكذلك قوله: توبوا إلى الله توبة نصوحا الآية، وأصله قوله: إن الحسنات يذهبن السيئات [هود، ١١٤/١١]. وبعد، فإن الآية لا تحتمل قول المعتزلة، لما هم يجعلون المصرّ على الذنب صاحب الكبيرة، ومن لا يصر عليه فهو تائب عنه نادم عليه؛ وفي ذلك أنه يغفر بالتوبة، وكل الذنوب تغفر بها والاثنان جريا بالتفريق من قوله: إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به [النساء، ٤٨/٤] الآية، وقوله: وإن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه [النساء، ٣١/٤] الآية. فهو - والله أعلم - أن الشرك لا يغفر إلا بالتوبة عنه، وغير أنه يجوز أن يغفر بالتفضل أو يُكفّر بغيره من الحسنات، ليصح القول مع تحقيق الفائدة لتمييز القرآن. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
They [minor sins] occur from the one who avoids major sins as forgiven, and in this is their affirmation, then expiation. And they [the Mu'tazila] make them forgiven not expiated, for the forgiven is that which is concealed, and in its remaining until a period of its removal. And the expiated is that for which a good deed comes from its doer that expiates it, like His saying: 'Those are the ones for whom Allah will replace their evil deeds with good deeds' [25:70], and His saying: 'Shall I guide you to a commerce?' [61:10] the verse, and His saying: 'If you give charity openly' [2:271] up to the mention of expiation, and likewise His saying: 'Repent to Allah with sincere repentance' [66:8] the verse, and its origin is His saying: 'Indeed good deeds remove evil deeds' [11:114]. After that, the verse cannot bear the saying of the Mu'tazila, because they make the persistent upon sin the possessor of a major sin, and whoever does not persist upon it is repentant from it and regretful of it; and in that, it is forgiven by repentance, and all sins are forgiven by it. And the two [verses] proceed with differentiation from His saying: 'Indeed Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' [4:48] the verse, and His saying: 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden' [4:31] the verse. So it is - and Allah knows best - that shirk is not forgiven except by repentance from it, and other than it may be forgiven by favor or expiated by other good deeds, so that the statement is correct with realization of benefit for distinguishing the Quran. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two types of forgiveness: maghfura (covering) and takfir (expiation). The author begins by stating that minor sins are forgiven for those who avoid major sins, then discusses the Mu'tazilite view that such sins are merely 'forgiven' (covered) rather than expiated. He explains that the forgiven sin is concealed and remains until removed, while the expiated sin is removed by a good deed. He cites several Quranic verses (25:70, 61:10, 2:271, 66:8, 11:114) to support the concept of expiation. Then he refutes the Mu'tazilite interpretation: they claim that the verse (4:31) shows forgiveness for those who avoid major sins, but the author argues that this does not specify the state of the persistent sinner. He concludes that shirk (associating partners with Allah) is only forgiven through repentance, while other sins may be forgiven by divine favor or expiated by good deeds.

Key Terms
مغفورة
مكفّرة
تكفير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that 'forgiveness' and 'expiation' are synonymous terms in Islamic theology.
Correction: This passage clarifies that maghfura means concealment of sin without immediate removal, while takfir means actual erasure through a good deed.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what distinguishes a 'مغفورة' (forgiven) sin from a 'مكفرة' (expiated) sin?
A) A forgiven sin is concealed and remains until removed; an expiated sin is removed by a good deed.
B) A forgiven sin is a minor sin; an expiated sin is a major sin.
C) There is no difference; both terms mean the same.
D) A forgiven sin is one that is repented from; an expiated sin is not repented from.

Key Takeaway
Maghfura conceals sin for later removal, while takfir removes it through a good deed, a distinction supported by multiple Quranic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your minor sins as merely 'concealed' until a later time, or do you actively seek to expiate them through good deeds right now? What specific good deed will you commit today to remove a sin you've been ignoring?
?
When you sin, do you assume it's automatically forgiven just because you avoid major sins, or do you feel the weight of its concealment until you actively remove it? How does this distinction change the urgency you feel to perform good deeds?
?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Interpretation of Forgiveness
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazilite view on forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite interpretation of forgiveness is flawed according to Imam Maturidi, and the correct position on expiation of sins.

Core Idea
Sins other than shirk can be forgiven by divine favor or expiated by good deeds, not limited to repentance.

In this passage, Maturidi directly engages with the Mu'tazilite view that forgiveness is tied solely to repentance and persistence on sin makes one a major sinner. He argues that the Quranic verses do not support this, and instead show that shirk is only forgiven through repentance while other sins may be forgiven by grace or expiated through good deeds. This lesson unpacks that refutation and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to follow the refutation.
Note the Mu'tazilite position as described by Maturidi.
2
Examine the contrasting verses
Identify the two Quranic verses (4:48 and 4:31) and see how they differentiate between shirk and other sins.
3
Understand the final conclusion
Maturidi concludes that shirk requires repentance, while other sins may be forgiven by favor or expiated by good deeds.
This corrects the Mu'tazilite view.

Third Aspect: Unspecified Penalties and the Principle of Equality
Maturidi argues against eternal punishment for major sins, using the principle of equality in recompense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that eternal punishment is not for all major sins but only for shirk and obstinate disbelief, based on the principle of equality in recompense.

Core Idea
Eternal punishment is only for those who persist in shirk and obstinacy, not for believers with other sins.

Imam Maturidi presents a third argument against the view that all major sins incur eternal punishment. He points out that the Quran specifies no exact penalty for major sins, but it does establish a principle of equality: one is only recompensed with the like of one's deed. This principle is key to understanding that the eternal punishment reserved for shirk and obstinate disbelief cannot apply to a believer whose sin is of a lesser degree. Let's read his reasoning and see how he defends divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is incomplete; the second sub-point cuts off. Focus on the first part.
2
Identify the principle of equality
Notice how Maturidi cites the Quranic verse (6:160) that one is not recompensed except with the like of one's evil deed. This is the foundation of his argument.
This principle is key to the entire argument.
3
Apply the principle to shirk vs. non-shirk
Understand that the like of shirk and obstinate disbelief is eternal punishment. For a believer without these, the sin is less severe, so eternal punishment would be more than the like of his deed.
This is the core of the argument against eternal punishment for all major sins.
4
Examine the first sub-point
Maturidi argues that punishing a believer like a disbeliever would make Allah's promise (of equal recompense) a lie, which is impossible.
This is a logical reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the incomplete second sub-point
The second sub-point begins to compare the magnitude of good deeds that counter disbelief and obstinacy, but the text cuts off. Consider how it might complete the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث أنه لم يبيَّن في الكبائر قدر العقوبات. ومعلوم أن الله نفي أن يجزى في السيئات إلا مثلها. ومثل الشرك والمعاندة إنما هو التخليد ولا ريب أن من ليس بمعاند ولا مشرك له في العبادة ذنبه دون من يفعل ذلك؛ بل صحة الاعتقاد في ذلك حمله على الخوف مما حذره ورجاء ما أطمعه في الاعتقاد، وهو الذي لو سبق كل شيء لكفّره هو ومحاه عنه. لم يجز أن يكون قدر ذنبه قدر الأول، فلم يجز أن يخلّد في ذلك. فيدخل فيه أمران أحدهما الكذب في الوعد حيث قال: ومن جاء بالسيئة فلا يُجزى إلا مثلها [الأنعام، ١٦٠/٦]، ومعلوم أن الكافر المعاند لو جمع عليه جميع ما يعاقب به على شرط النجاة والراحة يحتمله ويختاره. فثبت أن مثل سيئته هو الخلود في العذاب؛ فإذا لا يعاقب بما يرتكب وهو يعذب بمثله من كان ذنبه دونه كان جازيًا أكثر من مثله، وفي الحكمة عقوبته، وهذا مما يمنع الحكمة. والله الموفق. والثاني أنه معلوم أن الذي يقابل الجحود والمعاندة من الخير أعظم وأجل من الذي
And the third: that the extent of punishments for major sins has not been clarified. And it is known that Allah negated that one be recompensed for evil deeds except with the like of them. And the like of shirk and obstinacy is only eternal punishment, and there is no doubt that whoever is not obstinate and not a mushrik has, in worship, a sin less than that of the one who does that; rather, the soundness of belief in that carried him to fear of what He warned him and hope of what He made him desire in belief, and it is that which, if it preceded everything, would expiate it and erase it from him. It is not permissible that the measure of his sin be the measure of the first, so it is not permissible that he be made eternal therein. Then two matters enter into it: one of them is the lie in the promise where He said: 'And whoever comes with an evil deed, he will not be recompensed except with its like' [6:160], and it is known that the obstinate disbeliever, if all that he is punished with were gathered upon him on condition of salvation and comfort, he would bear it and choose it. So it is established that the like of his evil deed is eternal punishment in the torment; so if he [the non-obstinate] is not punished for what he commits, and he is punished with the like of that whose sin is less than him, then he would have recompensed more than its like, and in wisdom is his punishment, and this prevents wisdom. And Allah is the grantor of success. And the second: that it is known that that which opposes denial and obstinacy of good is greater and more majestic than that which
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage presents the third aspect refuting the Mu'tazilite and Kharijite claim that all major sins incur eternal punishment. The author argues that the Quran did not specify the exact penalties for major sins, and the principle of recompense is equality: one is not punished beyond the like of one's deed. The like of shirk and obstinate disbelief is eternal punishment, but for one who does not have such obstinacy or shirk, his sin is less severe. Sound belief (iman) creates fear and hope that can expiate sins. Therefore, it is unjust to equate the sin of a believer with that of a disbeliever, and thus eternal punishment is not applicable to the believer. Two sub-points are given: (1) A lie in the promise would occur if a believer were punished like a disbeliever, because the disbeliever's sin merits eternal torment, while the believer's sin does not—punishing them equally would violate justice. (2) The good deeds that correspond to denial and obstinacy are greater and more noble (text is cut off, but the argument likely contrasts the reward of believers).

Key Terms
الكبائر
العقوبات
المماثلة
التخليد
المعاندة
الشرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All major sins, regardless of context, lead to eternal punishment.
Correction: The passage clarifies that eternal punishment is only for shirk and obstinate disbelief. For believers without these, the sin is less severe and does not merit eternal punishment.

Checkpoint
According to the third aspect, why can a believer who commits major sins (without shirk) not be punished eternally?
A) Because Allah forgives all sins without any condition.
B) Because the believer's sin is not as severe as shirk, and punishing him eternally would be more than the like of his deed.
C) Because the believer repents before death.
D) Because major sins are automatically expiated by good deeds.

Key Takeaway
The principle of equality in recompense means eternal punishment is only appropriate for shirk and obstinate disbelief, not for other major sins.

?

?

?
The passage warns that punishing a believer eternally for a major sin would contradict Allah's justice. Does this give you a false sense of security, or does it drive you to greater gratitude that your sin is not as severe as shirk? What will you do to ensure your faith remains sincere?

The Injustice of Eternal Punishment for a Believer
Maturidi argues that eternal punishment for a believer is unjust due to outweighing good deeds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that eternal punishment for a believer would be unjust because it would nullify the greater reward for faith and violate the principle of just recompense.

Core Idea
Justice requires that reward for good exceed punishment for evil.

Maturidi tackles a profound question: if a believer commits sins, does he deserve eternal punishment? He argues no, because faith is the greatest good, and Allah's promise of tenfold reward for good deeds shows that justice demands a balance where good outweighs evil. In this passage, Maturidi presents a logical and theological case for why eternal punishment would be unjust.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the English translation. Notice how Maturidi sets up a comparison between the believer's good and evil.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two components
Maturidi contrasts the believer's good (faith) which is the greatest good, with his evil (sins) which do not reach extremity. See how he frames this balance.
3
Understand the principle of justice
Maturidi defines justice as reward exceeding punishment. Connect this to the idea that eternal punishment would nullify the reward for faith.
Notice the term 'jور' (injustice) is contrasted with 'عدل' (justice).
4
Relate to Allah's promise
Maturidi cites the Quranic principle: good deeds are rewarded tenfold, evil deeds only like. See how this supports the argument that eternal punishment is disproportionate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقابل ما كان في قبوله أن يفعل من الترك، على نحو ما كان الجحود والمعاندة من الآخر . فجاء بالذي هو في الخير أعظم الخير ، وفي الشر لم يبلغ نهايته. فإذا خلّده في النار أبطل ثواب أفضل الخيرات بارتكاب ما دونه من الشر. ومَرَدّ ذلك وصف الجور لا العدل. والعدل أن يزيد في ثواب ما جاء به على عقاب ما أتى به والله جل ثناؤه قد أخبر أنه يجزى الحسنة بعشرة أمثالها، والسيئة بمثلها ، وفي هذا لم يبلغ المثل في الحسنة ولا قصر على المثل في السيئة، جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى.
It parallels what was in its acceptance that it does from omission, just as was the denial and obstinacy from the other. So it brought what is in good the greatest good, and in evil did not reach its extremity. So if He eternally punishes him in the Fire, He nullifies the reward of the best of good deeds for committing what is lesser than it of evil. And the return of that is the description of injustice, not justice. And justice is that He increase in reward for what he brought over punishment for what he committed. And Allah, exalted is His mention, has informed that He rewards the good deed with ten times its like, and the evil deed with its like. And in this, He did not reach the like in the good deed nor limit to the like in the evil deed. Exalted is Allah above that and high.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that a believer who commits major sins deserves eternal punishment. Maturidi argues that such punishment would be unjust because the good of faith outweighs the evil of sins. He explains that justice requires that reward exceed punishment for good deeds, and Allah has promised tenfold reward for good deeds and only like for evil. Therefore, eternal punishment for a believer would violate this principle and be injustice.

?
