Three Answers to Al-Ka'bi's Use of Verses of Promise
Maturidi refutes Al-Ka'bi and gives three answers regarding verses of promise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's three answers to Al-Ka'bi's argument that verses of promise guarantee reward for any Muslim.

Core Idea
Divine promise depends on actual faith and God's grace, not mere words.

Imam Maturidi refutes Al-Ka'bi's claim that there is consensus on the unconditional application of verses of promise. After exposing the falsehood of the claimed consensus, he presents three distinct answers to show that reward is not automatic. This passage clarifies the nature of divine promise in the Maturidi school.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation.
Read the Arabic source and its literal translation to get the full context of Maturidi's argument.
Focus on the structure: claim refutation then three answers.
2
Understand the refutation of Al-Ka'bi's claim.
Maturidi begins by calling the claim of consensus a lie and cites Qur'an 5:85 to show reward is not for mere speech.
3
Examine the three answers.
Identify each of the three answers and how they undermine the idea of unconditional promise.
The first answer points to ultimate states, the second to actualized faith, the third to God's grace.
4
Reflect on the theological implication.
Consider how this preserves God's freedom and emphasizes human responsibility.
This is a key Maturidi principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : نقول وبالله التوفيق : دعواه الإجماع في عموم آيات الوعد كذب لا يزال يجعله مفزعا لنفسه في كل نائبة، وقد قال الله : فأثابهم الله بما قالوا جنات الآية، وليس يجب مثله للقول خاصة في قول الجميع، فثبت به كذبه في الحكاية. ثم لما استدل بالآيات أجوبة ثلاثة. أحدها الإخبار عن منتهى الأحوال، وإلى ذلك مرجع كل مؤمن. والثاني أن يكون الوعد للذين حققوا الإيمان بأخلاقه وما دل عليه؛ وجائز تسمية أحد بشيء، ذلك اسم لأمر تتصل به أمور عند جمع تلك الأمور، ويجوز عند إفراد الاسم بالذي به سمى. والله أعلم. والثالث أن يكون له ذلك الجزاء وما يعاقب به فهو يعاقب بحقوقه، وليس الذي يصيبه بحق دينه مقصرا عنه حق دينه. مع ما بين الله أن له الله فضلا كبيرا، ليعلم أن الجزاء للخيرات منه إفضال؛ وما كان حكمة الإفضال فإلى من يقوم به ذلك من اختيار أحوال وأوقات.
The shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: We say, and success is from God: His claim of consensus regarding the universal verses of promise is a lie; he does not cease to make it a refuge for himself in every calamity. And God said: 'So God rewarded them for what they said, gardens' [5:85], and the like of that is not obligatory for speech alone in the opinion of all. So his lie in the narration is established. Then, when he used the verses as evidence, there are three answers. First: They inform about the ultimate states, and to that is the return of every believer. Second: The promise is for those who actualized faith with its ethics and what it indicates; and it is permissible to name someone with something, that name being for an affair to which matters are attached when those matters are gathered, and it is permissible when isolating the name by that by which he was named. And God knows best. Third: He has that reward, and whatever he is punished with, he is punished for his rights, and what befalls him is not due to his religious duty, being deficient in his religious duty. Along with what God has made clear that God has great bounty, so that it is known that the reward for good deeds is from Him as grace; and whatever is the wisdom of grace, then it is for the one who undertakes that, from the choice of states and times.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Al-Ka'bi's claim that there is consensus that verses of promise guarantee reward for any Muslim. Maturidi first calls this claim a lie, noting that God's verse (5:85) speaks of reward for what was said, but this does not mean reward is due for mere speech according to all scholars. Then he presents three answers to Al-Ka'bi's use of such verses. First, these verses inform about the ultimate outcome of believers, not a guarantee for every speaker. Second, the promise is for those who realise faith through its moral qualities and implications; the label 'believer' only applies when its conditions are fulfilled. Third, reward is a grace (fadl) from God, not an obligation; punishment is for violation of rights, not for failing to earn reward. Thus, the verses do not prove unconditional promise for all who profess faith.

وعد
جزاء
إفضال
حقوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine promise is unconditional for any Muslim who professes faith.
Correction: Maturidi shows that promise depends on actualizing faith with its ethics and is ultimately an act of God's grace.

Checkpoint

A) Guaranteed reward for every believer
B) Ultimate states to which every believer returns
C) Immediate reward in this world
D) Only for prophets

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's three answers show that divine promise is conditional upon true faith and is ultimately an act of God's grace.

Graded Loyalty and the Weighing of Deeds
Maturidi explains the graded nature of loyalty and detestation and criticizes unjust cancellation of good deeds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that loyalty to believers is graded according to their rank in religion and that good and evil deeds are weighed separately without unjust cancellation.

Core Idea
Loyalty to believers is graded, not absolute; good and evil are weighed separately.

Imam Maturidi explains that our love and loyalty towards others should reflect their spiritual rank and deeds. He warns against an unfair approach that cancels out someone's good deeds with unrelated evil deeds. This principle is crucial for a balanced Islamic worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on graded loyalty
Read the Arabic passage carefully and notice how Maturidi lists the ranks and applies the principle to major sinners.
The passage begins with 'ثم الموالاة...' and ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله'.
2
Understand the weighing of deeds
Maturidi states that both loyalty and detestation are incumbent according to good and evil deeds respectively. Reflect on how this balances judgment.
The phrase 'على قدر ما أتوا من الحسنات، والمقت بما أتوا من السيئات' is key.
3
Consider the injustice of canceling good with non-opposing evil
Maturidi condemns those who try to repel good deeds with evil deeds that are not their opposites. Ask yourself why this is unjust.
The phrase 'بسيئات لسن بأضداد لهن' indicates that good and evil are not always directly canceling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الموالاة والتعظيم متفاوت على تفاضل المنازل والدرجات في الدين كالرسل ثم الأئمة ثم العلماء ثم المؤمنين، وعلى ذلك لازم الأمران جميعا في الذين ارتكبوا الكبائر؛ على قدر ما أتوا من الحسنات، والمقت بما أتوا من السيئات. فمن رام دفع ما جاء به من الخيرات بسيئات لسن بأضداد لهن فهو جائر في الحكم. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم احتج بالآية التي فيها يوم لا يخزى الله النبي والذين آمنوا معه في صاحب الكبيرة أنه لو كان مؤمنا لكان لا يعذب ولا يوعد عليه . والآية ترجع إلى وجوه. أحدها أن لا يُخزيه عن شفاعة رسول الله ، بل يشفّعه فيه وينجيه بها . والثاني أن يكون ذلك عندما يقول لهم تواهبوا مظالمكم، وعلى معذرتكم . والثالث أن يكون لا يُخزيهم خزى الكفرة من الخلود في النار؛ إذ هو انواع كما قال الله تعالى: ليس لهم طعام إلا من ضريع له [الغاشية، ٦/٨٨] ، وقال في موضع فليس له اليوم ههنا حميم ولا طعام إلا من غسلين
Then loyalty and magnifying vary according to the difference of ranks and degrees in the religion, like the messengers, then the imams, then the scholars, then the believers. And upon that, both matters are incumbent concerning those who committed major sins: according to what they brought of good deeds, and the detestation according to what they brought of evil deeds. So whoever seeks to repel what he brought of good deeds by evil deeds that are not opposites to them, he is unjust in judgment. And there is no power except through Allah. Then he argued with the verse in which is 'The Day when Allah will not disgrace the Prophet and those who believed with him' concerning the committer of a major sin: that if he were a believer, he would not be punished nor threatened with punishment. And the verse has several aspects. One: that He does not disgrace him by [denying] the intercession of the Messenger of Allah, but rather He intercedes for him and saves him thereby. Second: that that is when He says to them: 'Forgive one another your wrongs, and [accept] your excuses.' Third: that He does not disgrace them with the disgrace of the disbelievers of eternal abiding in the Fire, for it is of types, as Allah Almighty said: 'They will have no food except from thorny plant' [Al-Ghashiyah 88:6], and He said in another place: 'So he has no friend here today, nor any food except from pus.'
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is an argument and refutation. Maturidi first explains that loyalty (muwalat) and magnifying (ta'zim) are graded according to religious ranks: messengers, imams, scholars, believers. This applies to those who commit major sins as well: they receive loyalty according to their good deeds and detestation according to their evil deeds. It is unjust to cancel out good deeds with evil deeds that are not their opposites. Then he addresses an objection from the verse 'The Day when Allah will not disgrace the Prophet and those who believed with him' (Surah At-Tahrim 66:8?). The opponent argues that if a major sinner were a believer, he would not be punished at all. Maturidi gives three interpretations: first, 'not disgrace' means He will not deprive him of the Prophet's intercession; second, it refers to when Allah tells them to forgive each other's wrongs; third, it means He will not disgrace them with the eternal disgrace of the disbelievers, because there are different levels of punishment in Hell, citing verses about food of thorny plant and pus.

Key Terms
الموالاة
التعظيم
الدرجات
الكبائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that once a person commits a major sin, all his good deeds are nullified.
Correction: Maturidi shows that loyalty and detestation coexist proportionally; good deeds are not automatically cancelled by unrelated sins.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the ruling on someone who tries to cancel good deeds with evil deeds that are not their opposites?
A) It is permissible
B) It is unjust
C) It is recommended
D) It is obligatory

Key Takeaway
Loyalty to believers is graded and proportional, and it is unjust to disregard their good deeds because of unrelated sins.

Three Interpretations of 'No Disgrace' for Believers
Maturidi interprets the verse about no disgrace with three aspects to address the objection about major sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible interpretations of the verse 'The Day when Allah will not disgrace the Prophet and those who believed with him' and how they address the issue of major sinners facing punishment.

Core Idea
The verse does not promise total immunity; it specifies no disgrace through intercession, mutual forgiveness, or eternal shame.

Imam Maturidi addresses a potential objection from the verse that seems to promise no punishment for believers. He offers three nuanced interpretations that preserve both divine justice and mercy. This lesson unpacks those interpretations and shows how they apply to major sinners.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the verse interpretation
Read the Arabic passage from 'ثم احتج بالآية' to the end. Notice the three aspects Maturidi lists.
The passage contains three numbered interpretations.
2
Identify the three aspects
Maturidi presents three meanings of 'not disgracing'. Find each one and understand how it relates to major sinners.
The keywords are 'أحدها', 'والثاني', 'والثالث'.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Maturidi offers multiple interpretations. How do they together protect divine justice while affirming mercy?
The verse is used by some to argue that believers will never face any punishment. Maturidi shows it's more specific.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الموالاة والتعظيم متفاوت على تفاضل المنازل والدرجات في الدين كالرسل ثم الأئمة ثم العلماء ثم المؤمنين، وعلى ذلك لازم الأمران جميعا في الذين ارتكبوا الكبائر؛ على قدر ما أتوا من الحسنات، والمقت بما أتوا من السيئات. فمن رام دفع ما جاء به من الخيرات بسيئات لسن بأضداد لهن فهو جائر في الحكم. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم احتج بالآية التي فيها يوم لا يخزى الله النبي والذين آمنوا معه في صاحب الكبيرة أنه لو كان مؤمنا لكان لا يعذب ولا يوعد عليه . والآية ترجع إلى وجوه. أحدها أن لا يُخزيه عن شفاعة رسول الله ، بل يشفّعه فيه وينجيه بها . والثاني أن يكون ذلك عندما يقول لهم تواهبوا مظالمكم، وعلى معذرتكم . والثالث أن يكون لا يُخزيهم خزى الكفرة من الخلود في النار؛ إذ هو انواع كما قال الله تعالى: ليس لهم طعام إلا من ضريع له [الغاشية، ٦/٨٨] ، وقال في موضع فليس له اليوم ههنا حميم ولا طعام إلا من غسلين
Then loyalty and magnifying vary according to the difference of ranks and degrees in the religion, like the messengers, then the imams, then the scholars, then the believers. And upon that, both matters are incumbent concerning those who committed major sins: according to what they brought of good deeds, and the detestation according to what they brought of evil deeds. So whoever seeks to repel what he brought of good deeds by evil deeds that are not opposites to them, he is unjust in judgment. And there is no power except through Allah. Then he argued with the verse in which is 'The Day when Allah will not disgrace the Prophet and those who believed with him' concerning the committer of a major sin: that if he were a believer, he would not be punished nor threatened with punishment. And the verse has several aspects. One: that He does not disgrace him by [denying] the intercession of the Messenger of Allah, but rather He intercedes for him and saves him thereby. Second: that that is when He says to them: 'Forgive one another your wrongs, and [accept] your excuses.' Third: that He does not disgrace them with the disgrace of the disbelievers of eternal abiding in the Fire, for it is of types, as Allah Almighty said: 'They will have no food except from thorny plant' [Al-Ghashiyah 88:6], and He said in another place: 'So he has no friend here today, nor any food except from pus.'
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is an argument and refutation. Maturidi first explains that loyalty (muwalat) and magnifying (ta'zim) are graded according to religious ranks: messengers, imams, scholars, believers. This applies to those who commit major sins as well: they receive loyalty according to their good deeds and detestation according to their evil deeds. It is unjust to cancel out good deeds with evil deeds that are not their opposites. Then he addresses an objection from the verse 'The Day when Allah will not disgrace the Prophet and those who believed with him' (Surah At-Tahrim 66:8?). The opponent argues that if a major sinner were a believer, he would not be punished at all. Maturidi gives three interpretations: first, 'not disgrace' means He will not deprive him of the Prophet's intercession; second, it refers to when Allah tells them to forgive each other's wrongs; third, it means He will not disgrace them with the eternal disgrace of the disbelievers, because there are different levels of punishment in Hell, citing verses about food of thorny plant and pus.

Key Terms
الشفاعة
يوم لا يخزى الله النبي
غسلين
ضريع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves that no believer will ever enter Hell or face any punishment.
Correction: Maturidi shows the verse refers to specific disgraces: deprivation of intercession, lack of mutual forgiveness, or eternal damnation. Temporary punishment is not excluded.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is one valid interpretation of 'not disgracing' in the verse?
A) No believer will enter Hell at all
B) They will not be disgraced by being denied the Prophet's intercession
C) They will not sin anymore
D) They will be given a different name

Key Takeaway
The verse promises believers they will not face the ultimate disgrace of eternal damnation, but they may be punished temporarily and saved through intercession or forgiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused your sins by thinking 'I'm a Muslim, so Allah will save me anyway'? How does this lesson challenge that assumption and call you to take your sins more seriously?
?
The second interpretation mentions that believers will be told to forgive each other's wrongs. Think of someone you have not genuinely forgiven. Are you prepared to face them on that Day still holding a grudge? What step will you take today to release it?
?
If it is possible that you might face temporary punishment in Hell for your sins, does that change the urgency of your repentance? What sin do you need to repent from right now, not assuming you are safe from any punishment?

The Name 'Believer' for Major Sinners: Maturidi's Quranic Argument
Maturidi argues that major sinners are still called believers based on Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's Quranic argument that a major sinner retains the name 'believer' despite threats of punishment.

Core Idea
Threat does not strip the name 'believer' from one who has faith and good deeds.

In this lesson, we explore Imam Maturidi's defense of calling major sinners 'believers' based on the Quran. He uses the verse of retaliation and other verses to show that threat and punishment do not negate the title of faith. This was a contested issue among early theological schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi responds to an opponent's objection about brotherhood.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Examine the verse of retaliation argument
Identify how Maturidi uses the terms 'brotherhood' and 'believer' in the same verse to prove that religious brotherhood is intended.
3
Understand the reward and threat argument
See how Maturidi parallels the treatment of good deeds and threats to argue that threat does not remove the name 'believer'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does Maturidi end with 'there is no power except by God'? Consider the humility and reliance on divine judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في آية القصاص وما فيه من تسمية الأخوة : إن الله لم يُعدّ على الأخوة المطلقة ثوابًا ولا مدحا ، وإنما كان ذلك في الأخوة في الدين. فيقال له : قد سماهم مؤمنين في أول الآية، ثم أبقى لهم اسم الأخوة في آخرها ، ولا معنى سبق يحتمل صرف ذكر الأخوة إليه؛ ثبت أنه في الدين مع إبقاء اسم الإيمان وأما الثواب فقد شرط مرة باسم المطلق ومرة باسم المقيد؛ من ذلك قوله : فأثابهم الله بما قالوا . ثم قد يجوز عندك التحذير مع وجود القول وإن وعد عليه الثواب، فمثله المؤمن باسم الإطلاق. وقال : والذين آمنوا بالله ورسله أولئك هم الصديقون الآية ، وكذلك قوله : والذين آمنوا بالله ورسله ولم يفرقوا بين أحد منهم الآية، وفي صاحب الكبيرة يقال : آمن بالله ورسله ولم يفرق بين أحد من رسله، ثم جائز في مثله التخويف والوعيد. وكذلك قال الله : وإن تك حسنةً يضاعفها لدنه أجرا عظيما ؛ وصاحب الكبيرة قد أتى بحسنة، ويستحق ويؤتِ من الذي جاء به اسم الحسنة ، فما تنكر أن يستحق اسم المؤمن وإن أوعد. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And he said concerning the verse of retaliation and what is in it of naming brotherhood: God did not count for absolute brotherhood any reward or praise, but that was for brotherhood in religion. So it is said to him: He called them believers in the beginning of the verse, then He left for them the name of brotherhood at its end, and there is no prior meaning that would allow diverting the mention of brotherhood to it; it is established that it is in religion while preserving the name of faith. As for reward, He stipulated it sometimes by the absolute name and sometimes by the restricted name; from that is His saying: 'So God rewarded them for what they said.' Then it may be permissible in your view to warn despite the existence of the statement even if reward is promised upon it, so likewise the believer is [called] by the absolute name. And He said: 'And those who believe in God and His messengers, those are the truthful ones' — the verse — and likewise His saying: 'And those who believe in God and His messengers and do not differentiate between any of them' — the verse. And concerning the committer of a major sin, it is said: He believes in God and His messengers and does not differentiate between any of His messengers, then intimidation and threat are permissible in his case. And likewise God said: 'And if there is a good deed, He multiplies it and gives from Himself a great reward.' And the committer of a major sin has performed a good deed, and the name of the good deed is due and given to him from what he brought, so what prevents you from [admitting] that he deserves the name of believer even if threatened? And there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about whether a major sinner (ṣāḥib al-kabīra) retains the name 'believer' (muʾmin). Maturidi begins by referencing the verse of retaliation (āyat al-qisāṣ), which mentions brotherhood, and notes that God does not reward absolute brotherhood but only brotherhood in religion. The opponent claims that the brotherhood in the verse is absolute. Maturidi responds: God called them 'believers' at the start of the verse and kept the name of brotherhood at the end, with no prior context to restrict brotherhood to the absolute; thus it is religious brotherhood while preserving the name of faith. As for reward, Maturidi argues that God sometimes stipulates reward with the absolute name and sometimes with a restricted name, citing the verse 'So God rewarded them for what they said' (Q 5:85). He then argues that warning (takhwīf) and threat (waʿīd) can coexist with the statement of faith, just as a good deed (ḥasana) is still called a good deed even if it is threatened with being lost. Therefore, a major sinner who performs a good deed deserves the name of the good deed and likewise deserves the name 'believer' despite being threatened with punishment. He concludes with the phrase 'there is no power except by God.'

Key Terms
الآية القصاص
الأخوة في الدين
المؤمن
صاحب الكبيرة
الثواب
التخويف
الوعيد
الحسنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that threatening a believer with punishment means he is no longer a believer.
Correction: Maturidi shows that threat does not negate the name 'believer'; the Quran itself calls major sinners believers and threatens them.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi argue that a major sinner still deserves the name 'believer'?
A) By citing the verse of retaliation and the preservation of the name of faith.
B) By claiming that major sins do not affect faith at all.
C) By saying that the Quran never threatens believers.
D) By denying that major sinners exist.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Quranic name 'believer' applies to major sinners because threats of punishment do not cancel the reality of their faith and good deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your major sins do not cancel your identity as a believer? If so, why does the fear of punishment sometimes make you doubt your faith? Will you now hold firm to the name 'believer' even when you fall into sin?
?
Maturidi argues that threat and reward can coexist for a believer. Are you living as if you are both a believer and under threat? Or do you ignore the threat and assume you are safe? How will you balance hope in reward with fear of punishment starting today?
?
Your good deeds retain their name even if you sin. But do your good deeds actually reflect your faith, or are you relying on the name alone? What one good deed will you focus on today to strengthen your claim to being a believer?

The Wisdom-Based Distinction of Punishment
Maturidi begins explaining the wisdom-based distinction between eternal and non-eternal punishment, introducing two aspects and detailing the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the distinction between eternal and non-eternal punishment is based on the disparity of sins and what wisdom necessitates, not on arbitrary divine choice.

Core Idea
Punishment is determined by wisdom and sin severity, not arbitrary will.

Maturidi opens a new section on the distinction between sins that lead to eternal punishment and those that do not. He grounds this distinction in divine wisdom (ḥikma) rather than arbitrary will, emphasizing that God punishes according to the equivalent of the sin. This passage introduces the first of two aspects of this distinction, focusing on the inherent disparity of sins and the principle that punishment follows what wisdom requires.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text of Maturidi's argument, then follow the literal translation. Notice that the passage ends mid-sentence, marking where additional content follows in the original.
The page break causes an abrupt ending; the argument continues in the next lesson.
2
Identify the two aspects mentioned
Maturidi states that the distinction proceeds 'on two aspects' (على وجهين). Locate this phrase and notice that only the first aspect is explained here.
3
Focus on the first aspect: disparity of sins
The first aspect is 'consideration of the disparity of sins in themselves' (من طريق الاعتبار بتفاوت الذنوب في أنفسها). This means that the severity of the sin itself determines the type of punishment.
God promises to requite only with the like (مثل) of the sin.
4
Understand the principle of wisdom over choice
Maturidi argues that punishment occurs 'by what wisdom necessitates, not by what is chosen' (بما يوجبه الحكمة لا بما يُختار). Reflect on why he emphasizes this distinction.
This counters the idea that God punishes arbitrarily.
5
Connect to God's attributes of pardon and mercy
The passage ends by noting God's attributes of pardon (عفو) and mercy (رحمة). Consider how these attributes relate to the principle of just punishment.
The incomplete sentence suggests that these attributes prevent excessive punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر الفصل بين ما يخلد له العذاب ولا يخلد من طريق الحكمة، وذلك يخرج على وجهين. أحد هما من طريق الاعتبار بتفاوت الذنوب في أنفسها، وقد وعد الله أن لا يجزى إلا مثله ؛ وكذلك حق الحكمة، إذ التعذيب يكون بما يوجبه الحكمة لا بما يُختار، إذ ليس هو نوع ما يُختار وبخاصة ممن لا يضره الخلاف، ثم هو الموصوف بالعفو والرحمة، ولهذا ما أوجب
Then we mention the distinction between what incurs eternal punishment and what does not from the perspective of wisdom (ḥikma), and that proceeds on two aspects. One of them is from the perspective of consideration of the disparity of sins in themselves, and God has promised not to requite except with the like of it; and likewise the right of wisdom, for punishment occurs by what wisdom necessitates, not by what is chosen, since it is not a type of choice, especially from one whom disagreement does not harm. Then He is described with pardon and mercy, and for this reason [He] did not make [punishment] obligatory [beyond what is just].
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new section in which Maturidi explains the distinction between sins that lead to eternal punishment (takhallud) and those that do not, from the perspective of divine wisdom (ḥikma). He states that this distinction proceeds on two aspects (wajhayn), and here he elaborates the first aspect: consideration of the disparity of sins in themselves (tafāwut al-dhunūb fī anfusihā). God has promised not to requite except with the equivalent (mithl) of the sin, which is in accordance with wisdom. Punishment is determined by what wisdom necessitates (mā yūjibuhu al-ḥikma), not by arbitrary choice (mā yukhtār). This is because punishment is not a type of choice, especially from One whom disagreement does not harm (i.e., God is not affected by our opinions). Moreover, God is described with pardon (ʿafw) and mercy (raḥma), so He does not impose punishment beyond what is just. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the idea is that eternal punishment is due to sins that are grave and perpetrated in a state of disbelief or major kufr, while lesser sins may not incur eternity.

Key Terms
الخلود
العذاب
الحكمة
الوجهان
تفاوت الذنوب
المثل
العفو
الرحمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God punishes arbitrarily without regard to the severity of sin.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that punishment is determined by what wisdom necessitates and the inherent disparity of sins, not by arbitrary choice.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what determines the type of punishment?
A) God's arbitrary choice.
B) The disparity of sins themselves and what wisdom necessitates.
C) The opinion of scholars.
D) The sinner's own choice.

Key Takeaway
Punishment is not arbitrary; it is determined by the severity of the sin and what divine wisdom requires, reflecting God's justice and mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every punishment you have faced in life was exactly what your sin deserved, according to God's wisdom? Or do you secretly suspect God might be harsh? How does that affect your trust in His justice?
?
If punishment were by choice, you might hope for leniency. But Maturidi says it's by wisdom. Are you more afraid of God's wisdom or His choice? Why? Be honest.
?
The passage says God is described with pardon and mercy. When you sin, do you automatically assume His mercy will erase the consequences, or do you take seriously that He may require a just recompense? Which attitude leads to true repentance?

Good Deeds Outweigh: Why Major Sins (except Shirk) Don't Deserve Eternal Punishment
Maturidi distinguishes sins by wisdom, focusing on good deeds outweighing evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that a believer's good deeds—such as fear of Allah, hope for mercy, and trust in His generosity—outweigh the evil of major sins (excluding shirk), making eternal punishment for such sins incompatible with divine wisdom and attributes of generosity.

Core Idea
A believer's good deeds during sin outweigh the evil, so eternal punishment is unjust.

This passage from Imam Maturidi addresses a critical question in Islamic theology: Do major sins warrant eternal punishment? Maturidi argues that they do not, because the very act of sinning by a believer is accompanied by good deeds like fear and hope, which tip the scale in favor of mercy. This reasoning is grounded in Allah's attributes of wisdom, forgiveness, and generosity.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let the author's own argument unfold without interruption.
Notice the heading and the structure: 'two ways' and then 'aspects'.
2
Identify the two general ways
The passage opens by stating that distinction between sins is made through two ways. The first way is by considering the disparity of sins themselves. The second way is not elaborated here.
This lesson focuses on the first way and its first aspect.
3
Examine the first aspect: weighing good and evil
Maturidi explains that no one commits a major sin (without shirk) without at the time of disobedience acquiring good deeds like fear, hope, and trust. These good deeds, when compared to the sin, outweigh it.
The argument hinges on the presence of these concomitant good deeds.
4
Understand the implications for divine attributes
If the good outweighs, then punishing eternally would contradict Allah's attributes of generosity and kindness. This shows that major sins (except shirk) do not warrant eternal punishment.
Shirk is excluded because the disbeliever has no such good deeds.
5
Contrast with shirk
The passage concludes by contrasting the believer's case with that of the disbeliever (shirk). The disbeliever lacks any good deed deserving that name, so eternal punishment is consistent with wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ وجوب التفريق بين نوعي الذنوب بطريق الحكمة ]
ثم نذكر الفصل بين ما يخلد له العذاب ولا يخلد من طريق الحكمة، وذلك يخرج على وجهين.
١ - أحدهما من طريق الاعتبار بتفاوت الذنوب في أنفسها، وقد وعد الله أن لا يجزى إلا مثله ؛ وكذلك حق الحكمة، إذ التعذيب يكون بما يوجبه الحكمة لا بما يُختار، إذ ليس هو نوع ما يُختار وبخاصة ممن لا يضره الخلاف، ثم هو الموصوف بالعفو والرحمة، ولهذا ما أوجب
المغفرة والعفو عن كثير من الذنوب
ثم يخرج ذا على وجوه. أحدها أنه ما
من أحد الله بنوع من الكبائر دون الشرك يعصى إلا وهو لوقت العصيان مكتسب الطاعة من خوف عقابه ، والفزع عن مقته، ورجاء رحمته، [۱۷] والثقة بكرمه / وذلك عن خيرات لو قوبل بها ما ارتكب من الخلاف بغلبة شهوة وقهر غضب أو نحو ذلك ليرجُح ما كان منه من خير على ما كان من شر، فلا يجوز أن يُحْرَم نفعَ الخير ويوجب له عقوبة الشر، ومن ذلك فعله [فهو] موصوف بالجود والكرم، ولا كذلك معناهما ولا قوة إلا بالله وليس مع من يكفر بالله ويشرك به معنى يستحق اسم الحسنة والخير، لأنه يكذبه وينكر أمره ونهيه، فلا يحتمل أن يكون له الرجاء وفي دوام عذابه مضادة معنى الكرم والجود ولا قوة إلا بالله .
[The obligation of distinguishing between the two types of sins by way of wisdom]
Then we mention the distinction between that for which punishment is eternal and that which is not eternal by way of wisdom, and that proceeds in two ways.
1. One of them is by way of consideration of the disparity of sins in themselves, and Allah has promised that He will not recompense except with its like; and likewise the right of wisdom, because punishment is by what wisdom necessitates, not by what is chosen, since it is not a type of what is chosen, especially from one whom opposition does not harm, and He is described by forgiveness and mercy, and for this He necessitated forgiveness and pardon for many sins.
Then this is explained in aspects. One of them is that there is no one whom Allah [tests] with a type of major sins without shirk that he commits except that at the time of disobedience he acquires obedience from fear of His punishment, and dread of His displeasure, and hope for His mercy, and trust in His generosity, and that yields good deeds which if compared to what he committed of opposition due to overwhelming desire and suppression of anger or the like, the good he did would outweigh the evil, so it is not permissible that he be deprived of the benefit of good and that the punishment of evil be imposed upon him. And from that, His act is described as generosity and kindness, but not so the meanings of both [generosity and kindness apply to the believer]. And there is no power except through Allah. And there is not with the one who disbelieves in Allah and associates partners with Him a meaning that deserves the name of goodness and virtue, because he denies Him and rejects His command and prohibition, so it is not possible that he has hope, and in the eternality of his punishment there is a contradiction to the meaning of generosity and kindness. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. The author first states the obligation to distinguish between sins that lead to eternal punishment and those that do not, based on wisdom. He presents two general ways. The first way is through considering the disparity of sins in themselves. Allah has promised to recompense only with the like of the deed, and wisdom requires that punishment be by what wisdom dictates, not by arbitrary choice—especially for God who is not harmed by opposition. God is described by forgiveness and mercy, so He necessitated forgiveness for many sins. Then the author elaborates this first way into aspects. The first aspect: no one commits a major sin (short of shirk) without at the time of disobedience acquiring acts of obedience—fear of punishment, dread of displeasure, hope for mercy, trust in generosity. These are good deeds that, when weighed against the sin committed due to overwhelming desire or anger, cause the good to outweigh the evil. Therefore, it is unjust to deprive such a person of the benefit of his good deeds and impose only the punishment of the evil. This would contradict God's attribute of generosity and kindness. The author concludes by contrasting the believer who has such good deeds with the disbeliever who has no good deed deserving that name, because he denies God. For the disbeliever, eternal punishment does not contradict generosity.

Key Terms
التفريق (distinction)
الحكمة (wisdom)
الكبائر (major sins)
الشرك (shirk)
الخير (good)
الشر (evil)
الجود (generosity)
الكرم (kindness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any sin, including major sins, leads to eternal punishment in Hell.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that major sins (except shirk) do not warrant eternal punishment because the believer's concomitant good deeds outweigh the sin, and eternal punishment would contradict divine generosity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, why should a believer who commits a major sin (without shirk) not be eternally punished?
A) Because Allah forgives all sins.
B) Because the believer's fear of punishment and hope for mercy are good deeds that outweigh the sin.
C) Because the sin is not actually evil.
D) Because the punishment is only temporary.

Key Takeaway
A believer's major sin (except shirk) is accompanied by good deeds that outweigh it, so eternal punishment is incompatible with divine wisdom and generosity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a major sin you have committed. In that moment, were you aware of Allah's mercy and fear of His punishment? If yes, did you think those feelings could actually count as good deeds? If no, what does that reveal about the state of your faith? How can you cultivate that awareness before your next mistake?
?
This passage says that a believer's good deeds outweigh evil because of hope and trust in Allah's generosity. Is your hope in Allah's mercy alive even when you fall into sin? Or do you despair, thinking your good deeds are cancelled? What would it take for you to truly believe that your fear and hope are deeds that tip the scale?
?
The author contrasts the believer with the disbeliever: the disbeliever has no good deeds because he denies Allah. Your faith itself is the source of your good deeds. Have you ever treated your good deeds as separate from your faith? Starting today, how can you ensure that every good deed is rooted in a living, conscious connection with Allah?

Why Kufr Cannot Be Forgiven: Maturidi's Third Argument
Maturidi argues kufr is intrinsically ugly, so its punishment cannot be pardoned.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that kufr is intrinsically ugly (qabih li-aynihi) and therefore its punishment cannot be pardoned, unlike other sins.

Core Idea
Kufr is evil by nature; other sins are not—only kufr’s punishment is irreversible.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a decisive argument about the nature of disbelief. He distinguishes between kufr, which is inherently evil, and other sins, which are not. This distinction has profound implications for divine justice and forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms: قبيح لعينه, إطلاق, رفع الحرمة, عقوبة, عفو.
The text numbers this as 'the second' but it is actually the third argument in sequence.
2
Identify the distinction between kufr and other sins
Notice how Maturidi treats kufr and other sins (سائر المآثم) differently. For kufr, he says it does not admit release or removal of prohibition. For other sins, he says it is conceivable (جائز) to remove their prohibition.
The consequence for punishment follows the same pattern: punishment for kufr cannot be lifted; for others it can.
3
Reflect on 'intrinsic ugliness' and its implications
Consider what it means for something to be 'ugly in itself' (قبيح لعينه). This is a key concept in Maturidi's theology: some actions have an inherent moral quality that reason can recognize, and this affects what divine wisdom requires.
Unlike other sins, kufr can never become permissible, so its punishment is eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن الكفر قبيح لعينه، لا يحتمل الإطلاق ورفع الحرمة، فعلى ذلك عقوبته في الحكمة لا يحتمل الارتفاع والعفو عنه ؛ وسائر المآثم جائز رفع الحرمة عنها في العقل وإباحة ما له [من ] العقوبة، فمثله عقوبته. والله الموفق.
And the second [third]: that kufr (disbelief) is ugly in itself (qabih li-aynihi), it does not admit of being released and having its prohibition removed, so accordingly its punishment in wisdom (hikma) does not admit of being lifted and being pardoned; and all other sins may have their prohibition removed in reason and permission of what is of punishment, so its punishment is like that. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage advances another argument (the third in sequence, though the text numbers it 'al-thani' due to previous numbering). Maturidi states that kufr is intrinsically ugly (qabih li-aynihi), meaning its evil is inherent and not due to external factors. Therefore, it cannot be 'released' or have its prohibition lifted; in other words, it can never become permissible. Hence, in divine wisdom (hikma), its punishment cannot be lifted or pardoned. In contrast, other sins (sā'ir al-ma'āthim) are not intrinsically ugly; their prohibition can be removed in reason (i.e., it is conceivable that they could be made permissible), and thus their punishment can also be remitted. The conclusion is that the punishment for kufr is fixed, while for other sins it is contingent.

Key Terms
قبيح لعينه (intrinsically ugly)
إطلاق (release)
رفع الحرمة (removal of prohibition)
عقوبة (punishment)
عفو (pardon)
حكمة (wisdom)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can forgive any sin without distinction, including kufr, because His mercy is absolute.
Correction: Maturidi argues that kufr is intrinsically ugly, so in divine wisdom its punishment cannot be pardoned; other sins, however, are not intrinsically ugly and may be forgiven.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why cannot the punishment for kufr be pardoned?
A) Because God is not merciful enough.
B) Because kufr is intrinsically ugly and cannot be made lawful.
C) Because the sinner does not repent.
D) Because it is a major sin like others.

Key Takeaway
The intrinsic ugliness of kufr makes its punishment non-removable in divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever trivialized disbelief or thought of it as just another sin? After reading this passage, how does the intrinsic ugliness of kufr change your view of its gravity? What will you do to ensure you never underestimate the seriousness of disbelief?
?
Consider your own sins that are not kufr. This passage says their punishment can be pardoned. How does that knowledge affect your hope in Allah's mercy? Are you using that hope as license to sin, or as motivation to repent? Be honest with yourself.
?
If someone you know is struggling with doubts about Islam, how does this passage inform your responsibility towards them? What concrete step will you take this week to help them recognize the true nature of kufr before it becomes entrenched?

Forgiveness: Believer vs. Disbeliever
Maturidi argues that forgiveness for a kafir is misplaced as he denies the Benefactor, but for a believer it is fitting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that forgiveness for a believer is appropriate in wisdom but not for a disbeliever, because the disbeliever denies the Bestower of blessings.

Core Idea
Forgiveness is fitting only when the forgiven acknowledges the Giver.

Maturidi presents a subtle argument about divine forgiveness: it is not simply a matter of God's mercy, but also of the recipient's recognition. He distinguishes between a believer who knows his Benefactor and a disbeliever who denies Him. This passage explores the wisdom behind that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the literal translation. Notice the contrast between the two cases: the kafir and the one who commits other sins.
The Arabic uses a strong negation: 'لا كذلك' to emphasize the difference.
2
Identify the two groups
The passage compares two categories: the kafir (unbeliever) who denies the Mun'im, and the one who commits other sins (sā'ir al-ma'āthim) who knows the Benefactor.
3
Understand the reasoning for the kafir
Maturidi says forgiving a kafir is 'pardon in an inappropriate place' because he denies the Bestower and considers his denial correct. This would waste the pardon and nullify the blessing.
Key phrase: 'تضييع العفو وإبطال النعمة' - wasting the pardon and nullifying the blessing.
4
Understand the reasoning for the believer
The one who commits other sins knows the Benefactor, so he has a great status and honor. Hence, forgiveness for him is permissible in wisdom.
Contrast: the believer's honor ('أعظم الموضع') vs the kafir's denial.
5
Reflect on the implication
This argument shows that divine forgiveness is not arbitrary; it is tied to the recipient's acknowledgment of the Giver. This connects to the broader theme of divine wisdom (ḥikma).
Think about how this relates to the concept of justice and mercy in Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث أن العفو عن الكافر عفو في غير موضع العفو ؛ لأنه منكر المنعم ويرى ذلك حقاً، فيكون في ذلك تضييع العفو وإبطال النعمة ؛ ولا كذلك أمر سائر المآثم، بل يعرف صاحبها المنعم ، فله أعظم الموضع ، ولإكرامه أبين المحل، فجائز المغفرة له والعفو عنه في الحكمة. وبالله المعونة .
And the third [fourth]: that pardoning the unbeliever (kafir) is pardoning in an inappropriate place of pardon; because he denies the Bestower of blessings (al-mun'im) and considers that [denial] to be right, so in that would be wasting the pardon and nullifying the blessing; and not so the case of all other sins, rather the doer of them knows the Bestower of blessings, so he has the greatest place [of honor] and for his honor the most evident station, so forgiveness for him and pardon over him are permissible in wisdom. And with Allah is the help.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This is the fourth argument. Maturidi explains that forgiving a kafir is 'pardon in an inappropriate place' because the kafir actively denies the Mun'im (the Bestower of blessings, i.e., Allah) and considers that denial to be correct (haqqan). To forgive him would be to waste the act of pardon and nullify the very blessings that should be acknowledged. In contrast, one who commits other sins (sā'ir al-ma'āthim) does not deny the Benefactor; he knows who his Benefactor is. Therefore, he retains a great status and his honor is evident. Hence, forgiveness and pardon for such a believer are permissible in divine wisdom. The argument hinges on the recipient's recognition of the source of blessings: forgiveness is meaningful only when the forgiven acknowledges the favor.

Key Terms
عفو (pardon)
كافر (kafir)
منكر المنعم (denier of the Bestower)
منعم (Bestower of blessings)
حكمة (wisdom)
مغفرة (forgiveness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's mercy is unconditional for all, regardless of the recipient's attitude.
Correction: Maturidi shows that forgiveness is appropriate only when the recipient acknowledges the Giver. Forgiving a disbeliever who denies the Benefactor would be wasteful and contrary to wisdom.

Checkpoint
Why is pardoning a kafir considered inappropriate in wisdom?
A) Because the kafir does not ask for forgiveness.
B) Because the kafir denies the Bestower and considers his denial right, wasting the pardon.
C) Because God does not love the kafir.
D) Because the kafir has committed too many sins.

Key Takeaway
Forgiveness in divine wisdom is not unconditional; it is fitting only when the forgiven recognizes the Benefactor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself taking Allah's blessings for granted, acting as if you don't need to thank Him? Does that attitude make you, in that moment, more like the kafir described here—one who denies the Benefactor? What will you change today to genuinely acknowledge Him as the source of every good thing in your life?
?
When you sin, do you immediately turn to Allah in repentance, confident that He knows you acknowledge Him as your Lord? Or do you sometimes delay, as if His forgiveness is cheap? Remember that even for a believer, forgiveness is not automatic—it is based on your recognition of the Giver. Will you commit now to never let a sin pass without conscious repentance and gratitude?
?
This lesson says forgiveness for the kafir would be a 'waste' and 'nullify the blessing.' Does your own life reflect a deep appreciation for the fact that you are a believer—someone who knows the Benefactor? How would your daily prayers and gratitude change if you truly understood that your awareness of Allah is what makes His forgiveness meaningful for you?

The Fifth Argument: God's Favors Prevent Equating Believer with Disbeliever
Maturidi argues God's favors to believers preclude equating them with disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the abundant favors God bestows upon believers make it inconceivable for Him to treat a believer like a disbeliever, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite and Kharijite position on eternal punishment.

Core Idea
God's countless favors to a believer cannot be negated by sins, so equating believer with disbeliever is unfitting.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents his fifth argument for the impossibility of eternal punishment for believers who commit major sins. He describes the profound spiritual favors Allah has granted the believer—love for Him and His prophets, protection from belittling their station, and a deep commitment to the faith. Maturidi then argues that these immense blessings cannot be undone by sins that are minor in comparison, and therefore it is contrary to divine wisdom to punish a believer like an enemy of God, as the Mu'tazila and Khawarij claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the flow. Notice the rhetorical style and the use of Quranic and hadith references.
2
Identify the favors God grants the believer
Maturidi lists several specific blessings: love of God greater than love of this world and the Hereafter, reverence for prophets, protection from belittling them, and preference for God's friends over others. List these as you read.
These are the 'ihsan' (favor) that forms the foundation of the argument.
3
Understand the argument from magnitude
Maturidi contrasts the enormity of God's blessings with the smallness of sin. Ask yourself: How does the principle that God does not change a blessing until people change themselves (Quran 13:11) apply here?
The believer's sins are negligible compared to the blessings, so the blessings remain.
4
Observe the rhetorical question and hadith
Maturidi asks how God could gather the believer with enemies, and cites a hadith that everyone enters Paradise except those who refuse. Reflect on the force of this rhetorical device.
5
See the refutation of Mu'tazila and Khawarij
Maturidi explicitly rejects the sectarian claim that a believer who sins is equal to a disbeliever. He argues it is far from divine wisdom to guide one who prefers enmity and to deprive the faithful.
This connects the argument to a broader theological dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع أن يكون الله تعالى قد أحسن إليه في الدين في الوقت الذي جفاه هو بفعله : في أن جعل حقه أعظم في قلبه من الدارين ، وأنبيائه ورسله أجل في صدره من أن تحتمل نفسه الاستخفاف بشعرة من شعورهم أو الاستهانة بشيء من أمور دين الله أو الركون إلى من أعدائه فيها قد اختاره وآثره [بدلاً] من الخلان الله، وكل ذلك هو إحسان الله إليه وإنعامه عليه؛ فلا يحتمل أن يضيع مننه ويغير نعمه بجفوة يعلم أن قدرها من الذنوب لا يبلغ جزء / مما لا يحصى من نعمه عليه وإحسانه إليه، وهو يأمن خلقه بأن لا يغير نعمة أنعمها على قوم حتى يغيروا ما بأنفسهم ، وأن لا يضيع جميع ما أكرمهم به، فمثل الذي ذكر. وقد أنطق لسان رسوله أنه يدخل الجنة إلا من أبي ذلك . وهل يجمع بين من ذكرت وبين أعدائه ، مع كثرة مجاهدته إياهم في نصر دينه وإعلاء كلمته وقد ختم عليه؟ لا والله ما يفعل ذلك، وهو الغني الكريم، وهو العفو الغفور، وهو الرحيم الودود مع ما جاءت البشارة عن رسول الله في الحقوق العبيد لمن أحبوهم . ثم كان الذي ذكر أحب رسول الله، فيجعله قرين الشيطان ويحرمه زيارة رسوله ؟ جل ثناؤه عن هذا الوصف الذي وصفته به المعتزلة والخوارج ؛ على ظهور هذا الصنيع في جملتهم، حتى لا يسلم من ذلك أحد، ولعله لا يذكر عن خارجي أو معتزلي قبض سليما عن ذلك. وبعيد في الحكمة أن يوفق للصواب في الدين من آثر عداوته في شهوته وآيس من رحمته في أدنى منفعة، ويؤثر الخروج من دينه في مذهبه إشفاقا على نعمة يسيرة من الدنيا ويحرم من هو في ضد هذا الوصف. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the fourth: that Allah Almighty has favored him in the religion at the time when he [the believer] was harsh with himself by his action: in that He made His right greater in his heart than the two abodes [this world and the hereafter], and His prophets and messengers more exalted in his chest than that his soul could bear belittling a single hair of their hair or underestimating anything of the affairs of Allah's religion or inclining to His enemies therein, while he chose and preferred [them] instead of the friends of Allah, and all of that is Allah's favor to him and His blessing upon him. So it is not conceivable that He would waste His favors and change His blessings because of a harshness whose measure he knows from sins does not reach a part of what cannot be counted of His blessings upon him and His favor to him, and He assures His creation that He does not change a blessing He has bestowed upon a people until they change what is in themselves, and that He does not waste all that He has honored them with, so it is like what was mentioned. And He has spoken on the tongue of His Messenger that he will enter Paradise except the one who refuses that. And can He gather between whom I have mentioned and His enemies, despite his great struggle against them in supporting His religion and elevating His word, and he [the believer] has died in that state? No, by Allah, He does not do that, and He is the Self-Sufficient, the Generous, and He is the Pardoner, the Forgiving, and He is the Merciful, the Loving, along with the good news that came from the Messenger of Allah about the rights of servants for those whom they love. Then he whom I mentioned loved the Messenger of Allah, so would He make him a companion of Satan and deprive him of visiting his Messenger? Exalted is He above this description which the Mu'tazila and Khawarij have described Him with, despite the appearance of this action among them, so that no one was safe from that, and perhaps no mention is made of a Khariji or Mu'tazili who died sound from that. And it is far in wisdom that He would guide to correctness in religion one who preferred enmity towards Him in his desire and despaired of His mercy for the slightest benefit, and would prefer to exit from His religion in his school of thought out of compassion for a small worldly blessing, and deprive one who is opposite to this description. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This is the fourth argument in the author's numbering, but it is the fifth distinct argument overall. Maturidi describes the immense favors Allah has bestowed upon the believer: He has made love for Him and His prophets greater than love for this world and the hereafter, and has protected the believer from belittling the prophets or their teachings or inclining to enemies. All this is divine favor. Then Maturidi argues that it is inconceivable that Allah would waste these favors and change His blessings because of a sin whose magnitude does not compare to the countless blessings the believer has received. He cites the Quranic principle (Surah al-Ra'd 13:11) that Allah does not change a blessing until people change themselves. Moreover, the Prophet's hadith states that everyone enters Paradise except those who refuse (by disobedience). So how can Allah equate the believer who struggled for His cause with His enemies? He would not. Maturidi then refutes the Mu'tazila and Khawarij who claim that a believer who commits a major sin will be in Hell forever, equating him with disbelievers. He argues this is far from divine wisdom, since it would mean depriving the believer of what the disbeliever deserves and vice versa.

Key Terms
إحسان
نعمة
تغيير
البشارة
معتزلة
خوارج
زيارة رسول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believers who commit major sins will be punished like disbelievers in Hell forever.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's countless favors to believers prevent such equality; sin does not negate the fundamental relationship of faith and the blessings God has bestowed.

Checkpoint
What Quranic principle does Maturidi invoke to argue that God will not punish a believer like a disbeliever?
A) God is merciful to all.
B) God does not change a blessing until people change themselves.
C) Believers are never punished.
D) Sin always negates blessings.

Key Takeaway
God's abundant favors to believers make it unfitting to equate them with disbelievers in punishment, refuting the Mu'tazilite and Kharijite stance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have received countless favors from Allah—faith, love for the Prophet, and the ability to worship. Now think of a major sin you committed. Do you truly believe that sin can erase all of that? If not, why do you sometimes feel Allah has abandoned you because of your sin? What does this lesson teach you about hope and repentance?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that a major sin makes a believer equivalent to a disbeliever in punishment. Maturidi says this is unfitting because Allah's favors are too great. How does this change the way you view your own sins? Will you now repent with hope rather than despair, knowing that your relationship with Allah is not destroyed by sin?
?
You claim to love the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم. But have you ever belittled any of his teachings or underestimated the importance of following his sunnah? The passage says Allah made the Prophet's status so high that a believer cannot bear to belittle a single hair. Examine your actions: is your love reflected in your adherence? If not, what will you change starting today?

5 min
God's Grace Prevents Punishing Believers Like Enemies
Refutation of Mu'tazilah and Khawarij on God's treatment of believers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilah and Khawarij claim that God punishes believers for major sins, using arguments from God's grace, promises, and the principle that God does not treat a believer like an enemy.

Core Idea
God's grace prevents Him from treating a believer like His enemy.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a critical theological dispute: whether God would punish a believer who commits major sins in the same way He punishes His enemies. The Mu'tazilah and Khawarij argued yes, but Maturidi refutes this by appealing to God's abundant grace, His promise not to change blessings until people change, and the logical absurdity of equating the Prophet's beloved with a companion of Satan. Let's examine his arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid. Notice its rhetorical style and use of questions.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the verse references
The passage alludes to Quranic principles: that God does not change blessings until people change themselves, and the hadith about entering Paradise. Consider how these support Maturidi's argument.
3
Analyze the rhetorical questions
Maturidi uses two powerful rhetorical questions: 'Would He gather between those mentioned and His enemies?' and 'Would He make the one who was mentioned as beloved...a companion of Satan?' Reflect on the absurdity of the opposing view.
These questions show the logical consequence of the Mu'tazilah position.
4
Understand the final wisdom argument
Maturidi ends with a point about divine wisdom: it is far from wisdom to guide one who prefers enmity to God and to deprive one who loves Him. This underscores the inconsistency of the Mu'tazilah view.
The phrase 'There is no power except with Allah' is a closing supplication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنعامه عليه؛ فلا يُحتمل أن يضيّع مننه ويغيّر نعمه بجفوة يعلم أن قدرها من الذنوب لا يبلغ جزء مما لا يحصى من نعمه عليه وإحسانه إليه، وهو يؤمّن خلقه بأن لا يغير نعمة أنعمها على قوم حتى يغيروا ما بأنفسهم، وأن [ل] يضيّع جميع ما أكرمهم به، فمثل الذي ذكر. وقد أنطق لسان رسوله أنه يدخل الجنة إلا من أبي ذلك. و [هل] يجمع بين من ذكرت وبين أعدائه، مع كثرة مجاهدته إياهم في نصر دينه وإعلاء كلمته وقد ختم عليه؟ لا والله ما يفعل ذلك، وهو الغنى الكريم، وهو العفو الغفور، وهو الرحيم الودود مع ما جاءت البشارة عن رسول الله الله في الحوق العبيد لمن أحبوهم. ثم كان الذي ذكر أحب رسول الله، فيجعله قرين الشيطان ويحرمه زيارة رسوله ؟ جل ثناؤه عن هذا الوصف الذي وصفته به المعتزلة والخوارج؛ على ظهور هذا الصنيع في جملتهم، حتى لا يسلم من ذلك أحد، ولعله لا يُذكر عن خارجي أو معتزلي قبض سليما عن ذلك. وبعيد في الحكمة أن يوفق للصواب في الدين من آثر عداوته في شهوته وآيس من رحمته في أدنى منفعة، ويؤثر الخروج من دينه في مذهبه إشفاقاً على نعمة يسيرة من الدنيا ويحرم من هو في ضد هذا الوصف. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And His favor upon him; so it is not conceivable that He would waste His favors and change His blessings by a harshness whose magnitude from sins does not reach a part of the uncountable blessings upon him and His goodness to him, while He assures His creation that He does not change a blessing He has bestowed upon a people until they change what is in themselves, and that He does not waste all that He has honored them with, such is what was mentioned. And the tongue of His Messenger has spoken that he enters Paradise except one who refuses that. And would He gather between those mentioned and His enemies, despite his great struggle against them in support of His religion and exaltation of His word, and he has died in that state? No, by Allah, He does not do that, and He is the Rich, the Generous, and He is the Pardoning, the Forgiving, and He is the Merciful, the Loving, with what has come of the good tiding from the Messenger of Allah about the love of the servants for those they love. Then would He make the one who was mentioned as beloved of the Messenger of Allah a companion of Satan and deprive him of visiting his Messenger? Exalted is He above this description which the Mu'tazilah and Khawarij have described Him with, despite the manifest occurrence of this behavior among them, so that no one is safe from that, and perhaps no external or Mu'tazili is mentioned who died sound from that. And it is far from wisdom to guide to correctness in religion one who prefers enmity to Him in his desire and despairs of His mercy for the slightest benefit, and prefers to exit from His religion in his school out of fear for a small worldly blessing, and to deprive one who is in the opposite of this description. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilah and Khawarij who claim that God would punish believers with eternal punishment for major sins. The author argues by appealing to God's grace and mercy. He begins by stating that it is inconceivable that God would waste His countless blessings upon a person because of a sin that is minuscule in comparison. He then invokes the promise that God does not change a blessing until people change themselves, and the hadith that everyone enters Paradise except those who refuse. Using a rhetorical question, he asks if God would gather the believer and His enemies together despite the believer's struggle in His cause, and answers emphatically no, because God is Gracious, Forgiving, and Loving. He cites the hadith that a person is with those he loves, so would God make the lover of the Prophet a companion of Satan? He declares God exalted above such a description. He then notes that even the Mu'tazilah and Khawarij themselves are not free from sin, so their own logic would condemn them. Finally, he argues it is far from wisdom to guide to truth one who prefers enmity to God and despairs of mercy for a trivial worldly gain, while depriving one who is the opposite. The passage ends with 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Key Terms
نعمة (blessing)
منن (favors)
معتزلة (Mu'tazilah)
خوارج (Khawarij)
بشارة (good tiding)
حوق العبيد (love of servants)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's mercy negates His justice, implying that mercy and justice are contradictory.
Correction: Maturidi shows that God's mercy does not negate justice; rather, His grace treats believers differently from enemies because of their faith and love, which is consistent with His wisdom and promises.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of this passage?
A) God punishes believers and disbelievers equally.
B) God's mercy prevents Him from punishing a believer like an enemy.
C) God's justice requires eternal punishment for all sins.
D) The Mu'tazilah agree with Maturidi on this point.

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi argues that God's grace and mercy make it inconceivable for Him to punish a believing servant like His enemy, based on His promises, the hadith, and the logical absurdity of equating the Prophet's beloved with a companion of Satan.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own sins, do you truly believe that God's countless blessings upon you utterly dwarf them, or do you secretly feel that one sin can cancel all His grace? How would your daily life change if you fully internalized that God's mercy is always greater?
?
The hadith says everyone enters Paradise except those who refuse. Do you have an area of your life where you are 'refusing' by despairing of God's mercy or by acting as if your sins have made you His enemy? What would it mean for you to instead lean into His promise?
?
Imam Maturidi uses the rhetorical question: would God make the Prophet's beloved a companion of Satan? Think of someone you deeply love for the sake of Allah. Now imagine that person committing a major sin — would you instantly condemn them as lost? Or would you trust in God's grace? How do you treat yourself when you fall into sin?

Two Reasons Against Ignorance-Based Intercession
The shaykh gives two reasons why a previous interpretation of intercession is invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two reasons why interpreting intercession as involving estimation by ignorant beings is invalid according to Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Intercession does not depend on human estimation; God's knowledge and the records of deeds suffice.

Imam Maturidi now refutes a specific interpretation of intercession that would require someone to estimate the worth of the intercessor or interceded-for. He gives two clear arguments: first, such estimation would come from ignorance, while God alone knows the ultimate reality; second, the recorded deeds of every person are sufficient for divine judgment. Let us examine these reasons closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Notice the structure: the shaykh introduces two reasons with 'one of them is... and the second is...'.
Pay attention to the Quranic verses cited in support of the first reason.
2
Identify the two reasons
The first reason involves the impossibility of estimation by one who is ignorant. The second reason points to the sufficiency of recorded deeds and God's knowledge. Write them down in your own words.
Both reasons are presented as independent, but together they dismantle the earlier interpretation.
3
Understand how each reason refutes the interpretation
For the first reason, reflect on why relying on ignorant estimation is problematic when God is all-knowing. For the second, consider why the existence of written records and divine omniscience leaves no need for estimation.
Connect each reason back to the core idea that intercession cannot be based on ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } فنقول وبالله التوفيق الوجه [الأول] في الآخرة لا معنى له لوجهين أحدهما أنه في تقدير الأمر عند من يجهله والله جل ثناؤه هو العليم بحقيقة ذلك، بل غيره ممن يجوز عليه خفاء الحقائق كقوله : يوم يجمع الله الرسل فيقول ماذا أجبتم قالوا لا علم لنا ، وقال عيسى : ما قلت لهم إلا ما أمرتني به فكان [العلم] في ذلك عند الله ، وهم قد تبروا من العلم بذلك وأقروا بأن الله هو المتفرد بعلم ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله والثاني أن ثمة كتباً تُقرأ فيها أعمال بني آدم وما سبق منهم من صغير أو كبير، فهي الكافية في التقدير إن كان في حق الاحتجاج ؛ وإن كان في حق الإعلام فعلم الله بهم مغنٍ عن ذلك . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: We say, and with God is success: The first interpretation concerning the afterlife is meaningless for two reasons: One of them is that it involves estimating the matter with someone who is ignorant of it, while God, exalted be His praise, is the Knower of the reality of that. Rather, others whom concealment of truths is possible [are like] His saying: 'The Day when God gathers the messengers and says: What answer were you given? They say: We have no knowledge' and Jesus said: 'I only said to them what You commanded me.' So knowledge in that matter was with God, and they disavowed knowledge of it and acknowledged that God alone has knowledge of that. There is no power except with God. And the second is that there are books in which the deeds of the children of Adam are read, both small and great that preceded from them; these are sufficient for estimation if it is for the purpose of argument; and if it is for the purpose of informing, then God's knowledge of them suffices without that. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage is the shaykh's response to a previous interpretation of intercession. He begins by stating that the first interpretation regarding the afterlife is meaningless, and he gives two reasons. The first reason is that this interpretation involves estimating or assessing someone's status before someone who is ignorant of it, whereas God alone knows the true reality. The shaykh supports this by citing Quranic verses where the messengers disclaim knowledge of their followers' responses, affirming that God is unique in such knowledge. The second reason is that there are books (presumably the records of deeds) in which all human actions, small and great, are recorded. These books are sufficient for estimation if the purpose is argument; and if the purpose is mere information, then God's knowledge is sufficient. Thus, the first interpretation is baseless. The passage ends with the phrase 'There is no power except with God.'

التقدير

Misconception: One might think intercession requires human estimation of worth, as if someone needs to assess the merit of the intercessor or the person being interceded for.
Correction: Imam Maturidi shows that such estimation is impossible because the estimator would be ignorant of the full truth, while God's knowledge and the recorded deeds render any human estimation unnecessary.

Checkpoint

A) Because it relies on human estimation while God alone knows reality
B) Because intercession is only for prophets

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi refutes the interpretation of intercession that relies on human estimation by arguing that God's omniscience and the comprehensive records of deeds make such estimation unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you seek intercession from righteous people or rely on their status, do you truly believe that God’s knowledge of your deeds is insufficient? How will you put your trust in Allah’s all-encompassing knowledge today?
?
This passage shows that even the messengers disclaimed knowledge of hidden matters. Do you ever pretend to know things about others' salvation or spiritual status that only God knows? How can you stop assuming and leave judgment to the All-Knowing?
?
The books of deeds record every action, small and great. Which small deed have you neglected today that you will now perform, knowing it is written? What change will you make because God sees and records?

Why Intercession Is Improper for the Already Rewarded
Maturidi enumerates two reasons against seeking intercession for the already rewarded.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi considers seeking intercession for the already rewarded as improper, based on two rational arguments.

Core Idea
Intercession is only for sinners, not the already rewarded.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi continues his discussion on intercession by addressing a specific misapplication: seeking intercession for those who are already deserving of reward. He argues forcefully that such a request is logically repugnant and demonstrates ingratitude. Let's examine the two reasons he provides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice that the text is cut off at the end.
The argument is incomplete, but the two reasons given are clear.
2
Identify the two reasons
The passage lists two reasons why seeking intercession for the rewarded is repugnant. Find each reason and note how they are introduced.
Look for 'أحدها' (first) and 'والثاني' (second).
3
Understand the first reason
The first reason is that since it is not permissible to punish such a person, asking God not to punish is like implying He could be unjust or foolish. This is improper even for the wicked, let alone for God.
4
Understand the second reason
The second reason is that the appropriate response for a rewarded person is thanks and praise, not supplication for forgiveness. Supplication would conceal God's favor and show ingratitude.
The text cuts off mentioning that permission for such supplication is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الآية في الدعاء فكذلك نقول بدعاء لمن له ذلك الوصف ويُشفع له فيما كان في ذلك منه من المآثم والذنوب، لا أنه إذا كان أفعالهم ذلك فيشفع لهم لأنه لا يجوز في الحكمة تعذيبهم على ما ذكر من الأفعال، بل لهم عليها أعظم الثواب وأرفع المأوى؛ فطلب الشفاعة والمغفرة لمثله يقبح من وجوه أحدها أن ذلك إذ لا يجوز في الحكمة تعذيبه فكأنهم طلبوا منه أن لا يَجُور ولا يَسفَه ، وذلك لأفسق الخلق يخرج مخرج التفسيق، فضلاً من أن يتضرع [به] إلى الله، جل الكريم الحكيم عن هذا الوصف والثاني أن الحق في مثله " ـ إذ هو مثاب غير معاقب - [ أن] يلقى ذلك منه بالشكر والحمد، وفي الدعاء كتمان ذلك وكفرانه، ومحال الإذن في
As for the verse about supplication, we likewise say: [we affirm] supplication for one who has that description and intercession for him concerning the sins and offenses that were in that from him; not that if their deeds were such, they intercede for them, because it is not justifiable in wisdom to punish them for the mentioned deeds; rather, they have for them the greatest reward and the highest abode. So seeking intercession and forgiveness for someone like him is repugnant from several aspects: One of them is that since it is not permissible in wisdom to punish him, it is as if they asked Him not to be unjust nor foolish—and that, even when said to the most wicked of creation, would be an expression of wickedness—let alone that one should supplicate thereby to God, exalted is the Generous, the Wise above this description. And the second is that the right in the case of someone like him—since he is rewarded and not punished—is that he receive that with thanks and praise; and in supplication there is concealment of that and ingratitude, and permission for [it] is impossible...
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage is the shaykh's continuation, addressing the Quranic verse about supplication for intercession. He affirms that supplication and intercession are for those who have committed sins and offenses, not for those whose deeds are such that they already deserve reward. It is not justifiable in divine wisdom to punish the latter for those deeds; instead, they have the greatest reward. Therefore, seeking intercession and forgiveness for such a person is repugnant for two reasons. First, since it is not permissible to punish him, asking God not to punish is like asking Him not to be unjust—an improper request that even for the most wicked would be considered wickedness. How much more so when directed to God? Second, the appropriate response for such a rewarded person is thanks and praise, not supplication for forgiveness, which would imply ingratitude and concealment of God's favor. The text cuts off as the shaykh begins to say that permission for such supplication is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that intercession is always beneficial regardless of the recipient's state.
Correction: Intercession is only proper for those who have sins to be forgiven, not for those who are already worthy of reward.

Checkpoint
Why does the shaykh consider seeking intercession for the already rewarded repugnant?
A) Because it implies God is unjust
B) Because it shows ingratitude and concealment of God's favor
C) Because intercession is only for disbelievers
D) Because the rewarded do not need intercession

Key Takeaway
Seeking intercession for the already rewarded is improper because it implies divine injustice and shows ingratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If you truly believed that God's justice ensures the righteous are already rewarded, would you still feel the urge to intercede for them? What does that urge reveal about your trust in God's wisdom?
